⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ( إذا كان السواد أمراً واحداً في الخارج، ولم يكن له جزء فيه، بل في الذهن فقط يلزم أن يكون للبسيط في الخارج (صورتان ذهنیتان (متغايرتان ( تطابقان ذلك البسيط، أعني صورتي اللون وقابض البصر، ( وأنه محال بالضرورة ؛ لأن مطابقة إحدى المتغايرتين إياه ينافي مطابقة الأخرى له بديهة . قلنا : لا نسلم استحالته ) أي استحالة أن يكون للبسيط

We say: We do not concede its impossibility) i.e., the impossibility of it being for the simple...

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the view that the two attributes (color and sight-puller) are aspects of a single external entity. The objector argues: if blackness (sawād) is one thing externally with no external parts, then there would be two distinct mental images (ṣūratān dhihniyyatān mutaghāyiratān) corresponding to that single simple thing—the image of ‘color’ and the image of ‘sight-puller.’ This is claimed to be necessarily impossible because two different images cannot both accurately match one and the same simple entity. The author's initial answer is simply to deny the impossibility: 'We do not concede its impossibility.' The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating the full answer will be elaborated in the subsequent commentary.

Imaginary vs Intellectual Images: The Core Answer
Sanusi gives the central answer distinguishing imaginary and intellectual images.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single simple external entity can have multiple mental images that are intellectual and abstracted from matter.

Core Idea
Impossibility of matching applies only to imaginary images, not intellectual ones.

The objection claimed that if a simple entity had two distinct mental images, each would have to match it physically, which is impossible. Sanusi's answer hinges on the distinction between imaginary images (with magnitude, shape, and parts) and intellectual images (abstracted from matter). Only imaginary images cannot match a simple thing; intellectual images can, because they are extracted from the thing itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the passage where the answer is given. Focus on the contrast between 'ṣūratayn khayāliyyatayn' and 'ṣuwar ʿaqliyya'.
The author uses 'مطابقة' (matching) as the key concept.
2
Identify the two types of images
Notice that imaginary images differ in magnitude, shape, and arrangement of parts, while intellectual images are abstracted from matter and its accidents.
The difference is critical for the argument.
3
Understand the concept of extraction (intizāʿ)
The passage explains that intellectual images are 'muntaziʿa' (extracted) from the thing itself. If those images were concretized, they would be the thing itself; if the thing were in the mind, it would be those images.
This shows how multiple intellectual images can represent one simple reality.
4
Reflect on the concluding remark about parts
The author argues that calling these images 'mental parts' is not merely terminological; they are truly parts because they are extracted from the thing itself.
This counters a potential weak interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لأنهما في الخارج إلخ) فإن عاد المعلل وقال : المراد بقوله: فإن وجدا وجد كل واحد بوجود على حدة، تمنع الملازمة الثانية بأن نقول : لا نسلم أنهما إذا عدما أو عدم أحدهما أي لم يوجد استقلالاً لزم تقوم الموجود بالمعدوم؛ لجواز أن يوجدا بوجود واحد، أو نمنع حصر الترديد في الشقين. ولو حمل قول المنصف : أو نمنع الملازمة على منع ملازمة الشرطية الأولى والثانية بناء على أن التمايز بينهما ذهني، فهما موجودان بوجود واحد لا بوجودات متعددة انسد باب عود المعلل، ويكون لتأخير منع الملازمة وجه آخر وهو تعلقه بالملازمتين بخلاف منع بطلان التالي فإنه متعلق بتالي الملازمة الأولى . قوله : (قلنا إلخ ) حاصل الجواب : أن الممتنع مطابقة الصورتين الخياليتين أي الصورتين المتغايرتين في المقدار والشكل، ووضع الأجزاء لأمر واحد لأن مطابقتهما له يستلزم مطابقتهما المقدار والشكل والوضع، وأما مطابقته للصور العقلية أي المجردة عن المادة ولواحقها لأمر واحد فليس بممتنع؛ إذ مطابقتها إياه عبارة عن كونها منتزعة عن نفسه بحيث لو فرضت تلك الصور متشخصة بتشخصه كانت عين ذلك الأمر، ولو فرض حصول ذلك الأمر في الذهن بعد حذف مشخصاته كان عين تلك الصور إلا أن المصنف زاد في الجواب بيان كيفية الانتزاع بحيث لا يبقى فيه اشتباه . ثم لما كانت تلك الصور منتزعة من نفسه كان يقوم ذلك الأمر في الذهن بتلك الصور، فكانت أجزاء ذهنية فما قيل : إن تسميتها أجزاء مجرد اصطلاح لكونها منتزعة من نفس الشيء ليس بشيء. قوله : (أو) نمنع الملازمة الأولى تقديم منع الملازمة كما هو قانون المناظرة، وقد ذكر في بحث اللزوم من شرح المطالع أيضاً إلا أنه أخره خوفاً من انتشار الكلام فتدبر. قوله : (أو يقوم الأول بذلك الآخر وجه الاحتمال الأول أي قيام الفصل بالجنس على تقدير التغاير الخارجي وقوع الفصل نعتاً له، ووجه احتمال قيام الجنس بالفصل كونه مقوماً للجنس.
His statement: (Because they are in the external world etc.) Then if the reasoner returns and says: The meaning of his statement: 'If they exist, each exists with a separate existence,' you deny the second implication by saying: We do not concede that if they are both non-existent or one of them is non-existent, i.e., it did not exist independently, then the subsistence of the existent by the non-existent is necessitated; because it is possible that they exist with a single existence, or we deny the restriction of the disjunction to the two branches. And if the statement of the author: 'Or we deny the implication' is interpreted as a denial of the implication of the first and second conditional, based on the fact that the distinction between them is mental, so they exist with a single existence and not with multiple existences, then the door of the reasoner's return is closed, and there is another aspect for the delay of the denial of implication, namely its connection to both implications, unlike the denial of the falsity of the consequent which is connected to the consequent of the first implication. His statement: (We said etc.) The summary of the answer: What is impossible is the matching of two imaginary images (ṣūratayn khayāliyyatayn) i.e., two images that differ in magnitude, shape, and arrangement of parts, to one thing, because their matching to it would necessitate matching in magnitude, shape, and arrangement. As for the matching of intellectual images (ṣuwar ʿaqliyya) i.e., those abstracted from matter and its accidents, to one thing, it is not impossible. For their matching to it means that they are extracted (muntaziʿa) from its very self, such that if those images were individualized by its individualization, they would be that very thing, and if that thing were obtained in the mind after removing its individuators, it would be those very images. However, the author added in the answer an explanation of the manner of extraction (kayfiyyat al-intizāʿ) so that no confusion remains. Furthermore, since those images are extracted from its very self, that thing subsists in the mind by those images, so they become mental parts. Therefore, what is said: that calling them parts is merely terminological because they are extracted from the thing itself, is nothing. His statement: (Or) we deny the first implication, the priority of denying the implication as is the rule of disputation, and it was mentioned in the discussion of implication in Sharḥ al-Maṭāliʿ also, except that he delayed it out of fear of prolixity, so reflect. His statement: (Or the first subsists by that other) is a face of the first possibility, i.e., the subsistence of the differentia (faṣl) by the genus (jins) on the assumption of external distinction, where the differentia occurs as an attribute of it, and the face of the possibility of the genus subsisting by the differentia is that it is constitutive of the genus.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage provides the full answer to the objection that a simple external entity cannot have two distinct mental images. It begins by considering a possible counter-response from the objector: if the two attributes exist with separate existences, then the denial of implication would be contested. The commentator then explains how to block this by either allowing a single existence or denying the disjunction. The core answer distinguishes between imaginary (khayāliyya) images, which have magnitude and shape, and intellectual (ʿaqliyya) images, which are abstracted from matter. The impossibility of matching applies only to imaginary images; intellectual images can match one simple thing because they are extracted (muntaziʿa) from the thing itself. If the intellectual images were concretized, they would be the thing itself. The author provides further detail on the process of extraction (intizāʿ) to remove confusion. The images are called 'parts' in a meaningful sense, not merely terminologically. The passage ends with a note on the order of denial in disputation: it is better to deny the implication first, and an explanation of two possibilities for the subsistence of the differentia and genus. This lesson covers the complete answer and the subsequent clarifications.

صورة عقلية
إنتزاع
مشخصات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all mental images are similar and cannot represent a single simple thing.
Correction: The passage clarifies that imaginary images (with physical attributes) cannot, but intellectual images (abstracted) can, because they are extracted from the thing itself.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what kind of images cannot match a single simple external entity?
A) Intellectual images (ʿaqliyya).
B) Imaginary images (khayāliyya).
C) Both equally.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual images, being abstracted, can represent a single simple thing through extraction, whereas imaginary images with physical attributes cannot.

?
The passage explains that intellectual images are extracted from the thing itself by removing individuators. Disputation Order and Subsistence of Genus and Differentia
Sanusi provides procedural and metaphysical clarifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the strategic order of denying a claim in disputation and the two possibilities for how genus and differentia subsist relative to each other.

Core Idea
Denying the implication first is preferred in disputation; genus and differentia can subsist in two ways.

After presenting the main answer, the author adds technical notes on the proper sequence of denial in a dialectical exchange and on the ontological relationship between genus (jins) and differentia (faṣl). These insights refine the reader's understanding of how to defend the position and how attributes relate to a substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on denial order
Focus on the phrase 'تقديم منع الملازمة كما هو قانون المناظرة' and note the preference for denying the implication first.
This is a methodological point about debate strategy.
2
Understand the two subsistence possibilities
The passage presents two ways: the differentia subsists by the genus (as an attribute) or the genus subsists by the differentia (as constitutive).
This relates to the external distinction assumption.
3
Relate to earlier discussion
The author mentions that this was also discussed in Sharḥ al-Maṭāliʿ, and the delay was to avoid prolixity.
This shows intertextual awareness.

Multiple Mental Images for a Simple Entity: Objection and Answer
Objection and answer on two mental images of a simple entity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a simple external thing can have multiple intellectual images without contradiction, and why imaginary images cannot.

Core Idea
Two images can correspond to one simple thing if they are intellectual, not imaginary.

This lesson tackles a subtle objection: if something like blackness is simple (no parts externally), how can it have two different mental images? The objector says it's impossible. Sanusi's answer provides a crucial distinction between imaginary images (with physical properties) and intellectual images (abstracted from matter). Understanding this distinction clarifies how the mind can have multiple concepts of a single simple reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection that blackness as a simple external thing cannot have two distinct mental images. Note the claim of impossibility.
The objector uses 'color' and 'that which catches the eye' as two images.
2
Identify the answer's core distinction
The answer denies impossibility and introduces the difference between imaginary images (sura khayaliyya) and intellectual images (sura aqliyya). Understand what each type entails.
Imaginary images have magnitude, shape, position; intellectual images are abstracted.
3
Grasp how intellectual images correspond
Focus on the explanation: an intellectual image corresponds to a thing by being abstracted from it, not by sharing physical properties. The example of supposing the image individuated by the thing's individuation helps clarify.
4
Consider the concept of mental parts
Notice that the author says these intellectual images become mental parts. The criticism that 'parts' is mere terminology is rejected because the images are genuinely abstracted from the thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إذا كان السواد أمراً واحداً في الخارج، ولم يكن له جزء فيه، بل في الذهن فقط يلزم أن يكون للبسيط في الخارج صورتان ذهنیتان متغايرتان تطابقان ذلك البسيط، أعني صورتي اللون وقابض البصر، وأنه محال بالضرورة؛ لأن مطابقة إحدى المتغايرتين إياه ينافي مطابقة الأخرى له بديهة . قلنا : لا نسلم استحالته. حاصل الجواب : أن الممتنع مطابقة الصورتين الخياليتين أي الصورتين المتغايرتين في المقدار والشكل، ووضع الأجزاء لأمر واحد لأن مطابقتهما له يستلزم مطابقتهما المقدار والشكل والوضع، وأما مطابقة الصور العقلية أي المجردة عن المادة ولواحقها لأمر واحد فليس بممتنع؛ إذ مطابقتها إياه عبارة عن كونها منتزعة عن نفسه بحيث لو فرضت تلك الصور متشخصة بتشخصه كانت عين ذلك الأمر، ولو فرض حصول ذلك الأمر في الذهن بعد حذف مشخصاته كان عين تلك الصور إلا أن المصنف زاد في الجواب بيان كيفية الانتزاع بحيث لا يبقى فيه اشتباه . ثم لما كانت تلك الصور منتزعة من نفسه كان يقوم ذلك الأمر في الذهن بتلك الصور، فكانت أجزاء ذهنية فما قيل : إن تسميتها أجزاء مجرد اصطلاح لكونها منتزعة من نفس الشيء ليس بشيء.
For it is said: If blackness is one thing in the external world, and has no part in it, but only in the mind, then it necessarily follows that the simple [thing] in the external world has two distinct mental images that correspond to that simple [thing], namely the images of color and of that which catches the eye, and that this is impossible by necessity, because one of the two distinct images corresponding to it manifestly contradicts the other's correspondence to it. We say: we do not concede its impossibility. The summary of the answer: what is impossible is the correspondence of two imaginary images – i.e., two images differing in magnitude, shape, and position of parts – to a single thing, because their correspondence to it entails their correspondence in magnitude, shape, and position. As for the correspondence of intellectual images – i.e., those abstracted from matter and its concomitants – to a single thing, it is not impossible, because their correspondence to it consists in being abstracted from it, such that if those images were supposed to be individuated by its individuation they would be identical to that thing, and if that thing were supposed to occur in the mind after removing its individuators it would be identical to those images, except that the author added in the answer an explanation of the manner of abstraction so that no confusion remains. Then since those images are abstracted from it, that thing subsists in the mind by those images, so they become mental parts; so what is said that calling them parts is mere terminology because they are abstracted from the thing itself is nothing.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding whether a simple external entity can have two distinct mental images. The objector argues that if blackness (a simple accident) has no external parts, then having two distinct mental images (color and 'that which catches the eye') would be impossible because two distinct images cannot correspond to a single simple thing. The answer denies the impossibility and explains that the confusion arises from conflating imaginary images (which have magnitude, shape, and position) with intellectual images (which are abstracted from matter). Intellectual images correspond to a thing by being abstracted from it, not by sharing physical properties. The answer further clarifies that these intellectual images are indeed mental parts, and calling them 'parts' is not merely terminological because they are genuinely abstracted from the thing.

Key Terms
صورة خيالية
صورة عقلية
بسيط
انتزاع
أجزاء ذهنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two distinct mental images cannot correspond to the same simple external thing.
Correction: This is only true for imaginary images (with physical properties). Intellectual images, being abstracted, can correspond without contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the key difference between imaginary images and intellectual images?
A) Imaginary images are abstract; intellectual images have physical properties.
B) Imaginary images have magnitude, shape, and position; intellectual images are abstracted from matter.
C) Imaginary images are in the mind; intellectual images are in external reality.
D) There is no difference; both are impossible.

Key Takeaway
A simple external entity can have multiple intellectual images because they correspond by abstraction, not by sharing physical properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about a simple reality like your own soul, do you ever feel tension between the many ways you describe it (e.g., as knowing, willing, living)? How does this lesson's distinction between imaginary and intellectual images help you resolve that tension? Will you stop insisting that a single thing can only have one true description?
?
Consider a personal belief you hold that seems to have multiple intellectual accounts—perhaps a theological truth like 'God is one' yet has many attributes. Are you comfortable with the multiplicity of intellectual images of that single truth, or do you fear contradiction? What would it mean for you to embrace the abstraction process and let those images coexist without forcing them into a single imaginary picture?

Division of States into Caused and Uncaused
The author divides the state (hal) into caused and uncaused with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite division of states into caused (mu'allal) and uncaused (ghayr mu'allal), with key examples for each type.

Core Idea
States are either caused by an attribute or inherent to the essence.

This passage from al-Mawaqif introduces a fundamental classification in the Mu'tazilite theory of states (ahwal). The author distinguishes between states that are caused by a subsisting attribute (mu'allal) and those that are not (ghayr mu'allal). Understanding this division is essential for grasping the broader debates about attributes and quiddity in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the author's classification.
Notice the author uses 'first branch' to introduce the division.
2
Identify the two types
Locate the definitions of mu'allal (caused by an attribute) and ghayr mu'allal (not caused by a meaning).
The author gives clear examples for each.
3
Review the examples
For mu'allal: mutaharrikiyya and qadiriyya. For ghayr mu'allal: lawniyya, 'aradiyya, jawhariyya, and existence.
Existence is mentioned for those who consider it additional to quiddity.
4
Reflect on the significance
This classification shows how the proponents of states (the Mu'tazila) account for both derived and essential properties.
It sets the stage for arguments about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في تعريفات القائلين بالحال ذكر لهم فرعين: الأول : أنهم قسموه ) أي الحال ) إلى معلل أي بصفة موجودة ) قائمة بما هو موصوف بالحال، (كما تعلل المتحركية بالحركة الموجودة القائمة بالمتحرك، (و) تعلل (القادرية بالقدرة، وإلى غیر معلل هو بخلاف ما ذكر، فيكون حالاً ثابتاً للذات لا بسبب معنى قائم به، نحو اللونية للسواد والعرضية للعلم والجوهرية للجوهر، والوجود عند القائل بكونه زائداً على الماهية فإن هذه أحوال ليس ثبوتها لمحالها بسبب معان قائمة بها .
Concerning the definitions of those who affirm the state (al-hal), he mentioned for them two branches: The first: that they divided it (i.e., the state) into (a) that which is caused (mu'allal), meaning by an existent attribute subsisting in that which is described by the state, just as the state of being movable (al-mutaharrikiyya) is caused by the existent motion subsisting in the moving thing, and the state of being able (al-qadiriyya) is caused by power; and (b) that which is not caused (ghayr mu'allal), which is contrary to what was mentioned, and it is a state established for the essence not due to a meaning subsisting in it, such as blackness-ness (al-lawniyya) for blackness, accidentality (al-'aradiyya) for knowledge, substantiality (al-jawhariyya) for substance, and existence according to the one who holds it to be additional to quiddity. For these are states whose existence for their subjects is not due to meanings subsisting in them.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of hal (state) as understood by its proponents (the Mu'tazilites, particularly Abu Hashim and followers). It states that they divided states into two types: mu'allal (caused) and ghayr mu'allal (uncaused). The mu'allal state is one that is caused by an existent attribute that subsists in the subject. Examples: the state of being movable (mutaharrikiyya) is caused by motion, and the state of being able (qadiriyya) is caused by power. The ghayr mu'allal state is one that is not caused by any attribute subsisting in the subject; it is a state of the essence itself. Examples: 'lawniyya' (the state of being a color) for blackness, 'aradiyya' (the state of being an accident) for knowledge, 'jawhariyya' (the state of being a substance) for substance, and existence for those who consider existence additional to quiddity. These states are not caused by meanings subsisting in their subjects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all states are caused by attributes.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that some states, like lawniyya, are uncaused and are inherent to the essence.

Checkpoint

A) al-mutaharrikiyya
B) al-qadiriyya
C) al-lawniyya
D) al-mutaharrikiyya and al-qadiriyya

Key Takeaway
States are divided into caused (dependent on an attribute) and uncaused (inherent to the essence), with clear examples from the Mu'tazilite framework.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you treat it as a gift from Allah (caused) or as something inherently yours (uncaused)? How does the concept of uncaused states challenge you to see yourself as entirely dependent on Him?

Is it possible you have ignored the attribute that truly causes it, like the example of movability being caused by motion? Where in your life have you attributed something to essence when it actually has a cause you refuse to see?

Objection on the Stipulation and Its Answer
Objection and answer on the cause of states

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against requiring an existent cause for states is resolved by distinguishing the views of different kalam schools.

Core Idea
Not all kalam schools agree on the conditions for states.

This passage presents a sharp objection to the requirement that a cause of a caused state must be an existent attribute. If Abu Hashim allowed states to be caused by other states, how can this stipulation hold? The answer clarifies that the stipulation belongs to a different school, not Abu Hashim's, and that his own view restricts caused states to life and its consequences. This lesson unpacks the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by an answer, with a final note about Ash'arite view.
The passage uses 'fa-in qulta... qultu' format typical of dialectical reasoning.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection: how can Abu Hashim permit causation of a state by a state (ta'lil al-hal bi al-hal) while the matn stipulates an existent cause?
The objection targets a key condition in the theory of ahwal.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer has two parts: (1) the stipulation is on the school of others, not Abu Hashim; (2) Abu Hashim only allows caused states for life and what follows it, not for all attributes.
The answer shows that the objection is based on a misunderstanding of school differences.
4
Note the Ash'arite position
The passage ends by mentioning that Ash'arites affirm states like blackness-ness and movability, which are caused. This contrasts with Abu Hashim's restricted view.
This illustrates the diversity within the theory of ahwal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : جوز أبو هاشم تعليل الحال بالحال في صفاته تعالى فكيف اشترط في علة الحال المعلل أن تكون موجودة؟ قلت : لعل هذا الاشتراط على مذهب غيره. وقد نقل عنه أن الأحوال المعللة لا تكون إلا للحياة وما يتبعها، فإن غيرها من الصفات لا توجب لمحالها أحوالاً كالسواد والبياض على ما مر والمثبتون للحال من الأشاعرة يقولون : الأسودية والأبيضية والكائنية والمتحركية كلها أحوال معللة .
If you say: Abu Hashim permitted the causation of a state by a state in His attributes, may He be exalted, so how is it stipulated in the cause of the caused state that it be existent? I say: Perhaps this stipulation is according to the school of others. And it has been transmitted from him that the caused states are only for life and what follows it, for other than these attributes do not cause states for their subjects, such as blackness and whiteness as previously mentioned. And those who affirm states among the Ash'arites say that blackness-ness, whiteness-ness, being-at-an-atom (al-ka'iniyya), and being-movable (al-mutaharrikiyya) are all caused states.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection is: Abu Hashim (the leading Mu'tazilite proponent of ahwal) allowed that a state could be caused by another state (ta'lil al-hal bi al-hal) in God's attributes. So how can the author (the original matn) stipulate that the cause of a caused state must be an existent attribute? The answer: This stipulation is according to the school of others, not Abu Hashim. It has been transmitted from Abu Hashim that caused states are only for life and what follows it; other attributes like blackness and whiteness do not cause states for their subjects. However, the Ash'arites who affirm states hold that even these (al-aswadiyya, al-bayadiyya, al-ka'iniyya, al-mutaharrikiyya) are all caused states. Thus, the objection is resolved by distinguishing the different schools.

سواد
بياض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection shows that the theory of ahwal is internally inconsistent.
Correction: The inconsistency is only apparent; it arises from conflating the doctrines of different schools. Abu Hashim and the Ash'arites have different criteria for when a state can be caused.

Checkpoint

B) The Ash'arites' school
C) The school of others (not Abu Hashim)
D) All schools unanimously

Key Takeaway
The objection against requiring an existent cause for states is resolved by recognizing that Abu Hashim's school does not impose that condition in the same way as others.

The Second Branch: Equality of Essences and Differentiation by States
The author explains the second branch: essences are equal in themselves and differentiated by states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proponents' claim that all essences are equal in themselves and differentiated only by states, and the relative nature of this restriction.

Core Idea
Essences are equal in themselves; states make them distinct.

This lesson examines the second branch of the discussions on states. The proponents argue that all essences are fundamentally equal and that differentiation comes from states, not from the essences themselves. The author clarifies the scope of this claim and highlights an important condition about genera and species.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the concise structure: the claim, the clarification, and the exception.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the claim of equality
The proponents assert that all essences (dhawat) are equal in themselves. This means essences, considered apart from any attributes, are identical.
This is a foundational point for the theory of states.
3
Note the relative restriction (حصر إضافي)
The author clarifies that the restriction 'only by states' is relative. It does not negate other forms of differentiation, such as by privations or existent attributes, in the actual world.
This prevents a common misunderstanding.
4
Consider the exception about genera and species
Those who hold that genera and species are themselves states cannot accept the equality of essences, because that would imply differentiation by essences.
This shows the internal consistency of the position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثاني ) من الفرعين أنهم قالوا : الذوات كلها متساوية) في أنفسها، وإنما تتمايز) الذوات بالأحوال, أي لا بالذوات، والحصر إضافي فلا ينافي الامتياز بالعدميات والوجوديات حال الوجود واعلم أن القول بتساوي الذوات لا يتأتى ممن قال بحالية الأجناس والفصول كما لا يخفى .
(The second) of the two branches: they said: All essences are equal in themselves, and they are differentiated only by states—i.e., not by essences. And the restriction is relative (idafi), so it does not contradict differentiation by privations and existents in the state of existence. Know that the doctrine of the equality of essences is not tenable for one who affirms the statehood (haliyya) of genera and species, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents the second branch of the discussions of the proponents of states. They claim that all essences (dhawat) are equal in themselves, meaning they are identical in reality. They are differentiated only by states (ahwal). The author clarifies that this restriction is relative (idafi), so it does not negate differentiation by privations (adamiat) and existent attributes (wujudiyat) in the state of existence. Finally, it is noted that the doctrine of equality of essences cannot be maintained by someone who holds that genera and species are themselves states (i.e., ahwal), because that would imply differentiation by essences.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'equality of essences' means complete indistinguishability without any differentiation whatsoever.
Correction: The author clarifies that the restriction is relative; in the actual world, differentiation by privations and existent attributes is possible.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The proponents of states hold that essences are inherently equal and become distinct only through states, with a relative scope that accommodates other forms of differentiation.

The Objection Against States and Its First Rebuttal
Objection against states and its first rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that equal essences necessitate unjustified preference or regress, and the initial counterarguments that challenge its validity.

Core Idea
The objection against states is not decisive; regress in states is permissible and preference can be justified.

The theory of states (aḥwāl) faces a powerful objection: if essences are equal, their distinction by states seems arbitrary or leads to regress. The commentator responds by examining each horn of the dilemma, showing that neither is fatal. This lesson walks through the objection and its first rebuttal, clarifying the logical moves.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the commentator's response, then the author's summary.
The objection is presented in the first part of the passage.
2
Understand the dilemma
The objection presents two options: either the distinction by states is without cause (unjustified preference) or it has a cause, which leads to regress. Identify these two horns in the text.
The commentator later argues both are acceptable.
3
Examine the rebuttal of unjustified preference
The commentator says the claim of unjustified preference is debatable because multiplicity arises from states themselves, so preference is not baseless. Also, unjustified preference in states is permissible.
This is a key counterargument.
4
Examine the rebuttal of regress
The commentator states that regress in states is not impossible, so the objection fails on this horn as well.
This weakens the objection further.
5
Review the author's summary
The author concludes that the objection is weak because difference in concomitants with unity of substrate is impossible, so the whole argument is invalid.
This is the final refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبطله : أن الذوات المتساوية لا بد وأن يختص كل منها بحال حتى يتصور تمايزها بالأحوال، فإما أن يكون ذلك الاختصاص لا لأمر يقتضيه، وأنه ترجيح بلا مرجح وإما أن يكون لأمر، وذلك الأمر المقتضي للاختصاص إما ذات فالكلام في اختصاصه من بين سائر الذوات بالمراجحية أو صفة الذات فالكلام في اختصاص الذات بها أي بتلك الصفة وبالجملة فالاشتراك في الذوات أعني التساوي في الحقيقة يوجب الاشتراك والتساوي في اللوازم ضرورة، سواء كانت تلك اللوازم أحوالاً أو لا، قوله: (وإنه ترجيح بلا مرجح فيه بحث لأن التعدد في الذوات إنما حصل بسبب الأحوال، وبدون اعتبارها لا تعدد فيها، وهذا كاختصاص الفصول بحصص الأجناس والمشخصات بحصص الأنواع، وأيضاً الترجيح بلا مرجح في الأحوال جائز على ما بينه في التوضيح شرح التنقيح في مبحث المقدمات الأربعة. قوله : ( فالكلام في اختصاصه إلخ فإنها مساوية لسائر الذوات في تمام الماهية على ما هو المفروض . قوله : ( فالكلام إلخ ) . ويعود الترديد المذكور فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح، أو التسلسل وفيه أن التسلسل في الأحوال غير ممتنع ولضعف الاستدلال المذكور قال المصنف : وبالجملة إلخ، أي نترك التفصيل المذكور ونقول مجملاً في إبطاله : إن الاختلاف في اللوازم مع وحدة الملزوم محال .
And it is invalidated: that the equal essences must each be distinguished by a state (ḥāl) so that their differentiation by states can be conceived. So either that distinction is not due to something that requires it, and that is an unjustified preference (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ), or it is due to something, and that something necessitating the distinction is either the essence—then the discussion is about its being distinguished among other essences as the referent of preference—or an attribute of the essence—then the discussion is about the essence's being distinguished by it, i.e., by that attribute. In sum, the commonality in the essences, meaning equality in reality, necessarily entails commonality and equality in the necessary concomitants (lawāzim), whether those concomitants are states or not. His statement: 'And it is an unjustified preference' contains a discussion, because the multiplicity in the essences only came about due to the states, and without considering them there is no multiplicity in them, and this is like the distinction of differentiae (fuṣūl) by the shares of genera (aǧnās) and of individuators (mušakhkhiṣāt) by the shares of species (anwāʿ). Also, unjustified preference in states is permissible, as explained in al-Tawḍīḥ Sharḥ al-Tanqīḥ in the discussion of the four premises. His statement: 'Then the discussion is about its distinction, etc.' — for it is equal to other essences in the complete quiddity (māhiyya), as assumed. His statement: 'Then the discussion, etc.' And the mentioned disjunction returns, necessitating either unjustified preference or regress (tasalsul). There is a response: regress in states is not impossible. And due to the weakness of the mentioned argument, the author said: 'In sum, etc.,' i.e., we leave aside the detailed discussion and say summarily in refuting it: difference in concomitants with unity of the substrate (malzūm) is impossible.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an objection and the commentator's initial rebuttal concerning the theory of states (aḥwāl). The objection states that if essences (dhawāt) are equal in reality, they must be distinguished by states; this distinction either occurs without a cause (unjustified preference) or with a cause, leading to a regress. The commentator first notes that the claim of unjustified preference is debatable: multiplicity in essences is due to states, so preference is not baseless; rather, it is like the distinction of differentiae by genera shares. He also points out that the regress in states is not impossible. The author (al-Muṣannif) then concludes that the objection is weak, because difference in concomitants (lawāzim) with unity of the substrate is impossible, so the entire argument fails.

Key Terms
الذوات
الأحوال
الترجيح بلا مرجح
التسلسل
اللوازم
الماهية
الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection decisively refutes the theory of states because it leads to unjustified preference or regress.
Correction: The commentator shows that unjustified preference is not necessarily invalid (it is analogous to other distinctions) and regress in states is permissible, so the objection is not decisive.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is the claim of 'unjustified preference' not a valid objection against the theory of states?
A) Because all preferences require a cause.
B) Because multiplicity in essences is due to states, so the preference is not baseless.
C) Because the objection itself is circular.
D) Because the author admitted the objection is strong.

Key Takeaway
The objection that states lead to unjustified preference or regress is not decisive, because both horns can be rebutted: preference is not baseless and regress in states is permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often make arguments from preference or causality. When you encounter a dilemma like the one here about God's attributes, do you quickly assume it is decisive, or do you carefully examine each horn as the commentator does? Are you willing to consider that what seems like an unjustified preference might actually have a basis you haven't considered? What change in your intellectual humility is required?
?
The commentator allows regress in states because it is not impossible. In your own life, do you reject possibilities simply because they involve an infinite series, without checking whether such regress is actually impossible? How might this lesson affect your approach to theological reasoning, especially regarding divine attributes?
?
The summary refutation says: difference in concomitants with unity of substrate is impossible. Consider your own self: your essence is one, yet you have many states (emotions, thoughts). How do you reconcile the unity of your self with the diversity of your states? Does this lesson change how you think about the relationship between God's essence and His attributes? What practical difference does this make in your worship or understanding of God?

Clarifying 'Equality in Reality' and the Phrase 'Must Be Distinguished'
Commentary on two phrases: 'equality in reality' and 'must be distinguished'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator interprets 'equality in reality' to mean that commonality in essence does not entail commonality in necessary concomitants, and the grammatical nuance of the phrase 'must be distinguished'.

Core Idea
Commonality in essence does not force commonality in all attributes.

Sanusi's commentator begins by unpacking two key phrases from the original text. 'Equality in reality' is explained as a restricted form of commonality that does not extend to the necessary concomitants (al-lawāzim). Then, the grammatical function of the wāw in 'lā budda wa-an yakhtaṣṣ' is analyzed, showing either a conjunctive with an implied verb or an extra wāw for emphasis. These clarifications set the stage for the subsequent discussion on states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on 'equality in reality'
Focus on the phrase 'aʿnī al-tasāwī fī al-ḥaqīqa' and its explanation. Notice how the commentator distinguishes between absolute commonality and commonality in reality.
The key idea is that absolute commonality (muṭlaq al-ishtirāk) does not require shared concomitants.
2
Examine the grammatical analysis of 'lā budda wa-an yakhtaṣṣ'
The commentator presents two interpretations: the wāw is conjunctive with an implied verb, or it is extra for emphasis. Consider how this affects meaning.
This is a technical grammatical point typical of the sharh tradition.
3
Reflect on the connection to kalam
Why does this matter? The clarification about commonality and concomitants is crucial for the later argument about states (aḥwāl) and the distinction of essences.
The commentator is laying groundwork for the main argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( أعني التساوي في الحقيقة فسر الاشتراك بالتساوي بالحقيقة، إذ مطلقه لا يوجب الاشتراك في اللوازم . قوله لا بد وأن يختص إلخ) أي لا بد أن يمتاز ويختص فالواو عاطفة على المقدر. وقيل : الواو زائدة في خبر لا لتأكيد اللصوق لا للعطف على المقدر، وقس على ما ذكرته نظائر هذا التركيب . قوله : ( فالكلام في اختصاص الذات بها فيه بحث لما سيذكره في الجواب الأول أنهم يلتزمون التسلسل في الأحوال ويشير هناك إلى أن رد الرازي مندفع عنهم، فلقائل أن يقول : يجوز عندهم أن يكون اختصاص كل ذات بحال أخرى لا إلى نهاية فلا يلزمهم الترجيح بلا مرجح ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأن الأحوال الغير المتناهية إن حصل لكل ذات لم يبق الاختصاص المفروض، وإلا لم يكن الاشتراك في الملزوم ملزوماً للاشتراك في اللازم، وكل منهما محال، والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال . قوله : ( فالاشتراك في الذوات الظاهر أن المراد بالذوات الخصوصيات، والظرف مستقر أي الاشتراك الكائن في الذوات وقوله : أعني التساوي في الحقيقة بالنظر إلى مآل المعنى، وقد فكيف يتصور الاشتراك والتساوي في الحقيقة مع الامتياز باللوازم التي هي الأحوال ؟
His statement: 'I mean equality in reality' — he explained 'commonality' as equality in reality, because its absolute form does not necessitate commonality in the necessary concomitants. His statement: 'must and be distinguished, etc.' — meaning: must be distinguished and specified, so the wāw is conjunctive with an implied [verb]. And it is said: the wāw is extra in the predicate of 'lā' to emphasize connection, not a conjunctive with the implied. And analogize to what I have mentioned the parallels of this construction. His statement: 'Then the discussion is about the essence's being distinguished by it' requires investigation because he will mention in the first answer that they (the proponents of states) commit to regress in states, and he indicates there that the refutation of al-Rāzī is repelled from them. So one might say: It is permissible according to them that the distinction of each essence by another state goes on ad infinitum, so they are not forced into unjustified preference. And it can be answered: If the infinite states accrue to each essence, the supposed distinction would not remain; otherwise, commonality in the substrate would not necessitate commonality in the necessary concomitant, and both are impossible. And God knows the truth of the matter. His statement: 'Then the commonality in the essences' — apparently what is meant by essences is the particularities, and the adverbial phrase is an implied predicate, i.e., the commonality existing in the essences. And his statement: 'I mean equality in reality' is with respect to the final import of the meaning. And it has been [said]: How can commonality and equality in reality be conceived together with distinction by the concomitants which are the states?
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on specific phrases from the original text. The commentator first clarifies the phrase 'I mean equality in reality' (aʿnī al-tasāwī fī al-ḥaqīqa), explaining that 'commonality' (ishtirāk) is being interpreted as equality in reality because absolute commonality does not force commonality in concomitants. Then he examines the phrase 'must be distinguished' (lā budda wa-an yakhtaṣṣ), discussing grammatical points: the wāw is conjunctive with an implied verb, or some say it is extra for emphasis. Next, he critiques the statement 'the discussion is about the essence's being distinguished by it' (al-kalām fī ikhtiṣāṣ al-dhāt bihā) by noting that the proponents of states accept infinite regress in states, which al-Rāzī's objection cannot refute. A possible objection is raised: if each essence has infinite states, the distinguishing function collapses, but the commentator notes that either the infinite states preserve difference or they do not—both possibilities lead to contradiction. He ends with a rhetorical question: how can commonality in reality coexist with distinction by the concomitants that are the states? This question transitions to the next section on the deniers' view.

Key Terms
الاشتراك
التساوي
اللوازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'equality in reality' means absolute identity in all respects.
Correction: The commentator specifies it only concerns commonality in essence, not in the necessary concomitants (lawāzim).

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is commonality explained as 'equality in reality'?
A) Because absolute commonality does not necessitate commonality in the necessary concomitants.
B) Because all essences are identical.
C) Because the wāw is conjunctive.
D) Because the commentator disagrees with the author.

Key Takeaway
Commonality in essence does not imply commonality in all attributes; this is a key distinction for arguments about the states (aḥwāl).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at others who share your faith or humanity, do you assume they must share all your beliefs or characteristics? How does the lesson's distinction between common essence and attributes challenge you to avoid false assumptions in your relationships? The commentator warns against misinterpreting grammatical structures in theological arguments. Think of a time you misunderstood a statement about Allah because you assumed a certain grammatical relationship. How can you cultivate humility and care in interpreting Islamic texts? What is the consequence of getting it wrong?
?
If Allah's existence is common to creation (since He gives existence), how might you be tempted to think He shares other attributes with creation? How does this lesson protect tawhid by clarifying that commonality in essence does not mean commonality in attributes? The Regress Objection and Its Resolution
Discussion of the regress objection and its resolution, leading to a concluding question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator presents and resolves the objection that infinite regress in states undermines the distinction of essences.

Core Idea
Infinite states are permissible but lead to a contradiction if not properly handled.

The commentator now tackles a challenging objection: if each essence has an infinite series of distinguishing states, doesn't that destroy the very distinction it is meant to explain? He presents both a possible objection and a counter-response, showing that the proponents of states can avoid al-Rāzī's refutation. The passage ends with a rhetorical question that transitions to the deniers' perspective.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about regress in states
Identify the core concern: that infinite states for each essence would eliminate the supposed distinction between essences.
This is a classic objection in the debate on states (aḥwāl).
2
Examine the possible answer from the proponents
The commentator notes that the proponents of states accept infinite regress. They argue that distinction by another state ad infinitum does not force an unjustified preference (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ).
This shows that infinite regress is not automatically impossible in their view.
3
Understand the commentator's counter-response
The commentator then offers a rebuttal: if infinite states accrue to each essence, the distinction would not remain (because each essence would have all states), otherwise commonality in substrate would not necessitate commonality in concomitant—both consequences are impossible.
This is a deeper critique, showing the objection is not fatal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is a possible answer to the objection that infinite states eliminate the supposed distinction?
A) Infinite states are impossible, so the objection stands.
B) The distinction remains because essences are different in themselves.
C) If infinite states accrue to each essence, the distinction would not remain, and the argument would collapse; this is a direct answer showing the objection is not fatal.
D) The commentator admits the objection is valid.

Key Takeaway
The regress objection is not fatal; the proponents can accept infinite states and still maintain their position, but the commentator raises a deeper issue with the rhetorical question.

The Deniers' View: Essences Are Essentially Different
The deniers state their own view on essences and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deniers' (nufāt al-aḥwāl) positive view that essences are inherently different in their realities yet can share necessary attributes without logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Essences are not all alike; their differences are real, but common attributes can still arise from different realities.

The deniers of states begin by stating their own position: essences are not the same in their fundamental realities. They can, however, share certain necessary properties. This is not problematic. In contrast, the proponents' view that essences are identical yet have contradictory properties is impossible. This lesson sets the stage for the subsequent refutation of the proponents' argument for states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the deniers' claim.
2
Identify the deniers' claim about essences
Note that they say essences are 'mutually different in realities'.
3
Understand the permissibility of sharing concomitants
Different realities can necessitate the same concomitant, so sharing attributes is not impossible.
4
Contrast with the proponents' view
The proponents view essences as equal yet with differing concomitants, which the deniers say is impossible.
5
Reflect on why this matters
This foundation makes the deniers reject the need for an intermediate 'state'.
It sets up their refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما على رأينا يعني نفاة الأحوال : فالذوات متخالفة في الحقائق، وأنها تشترك في اللوازم وذلك غير ممتنع ؛ لجواز أن تكون الحقائق المختلفة مقتضية لأمر واحد لازم لها بخلاف العكس وهو أن تكون الذوات مشتركة متساوية مع الاختلاف والتنافي في اللوازم كما هو رأيكم فإنه ممتنع قطعاً . وربما قال النافون للأحوال : إن ملخص حجة المثبتين لها هو أن الحقائق مشتركة في أمور، ومختلفة بخصوصياتها، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الاختلاف وهما ليسا بموجودين ولا معدومين فقد ثبت الواسطة التي هي الحال، وذلك منقوض بأن الأحوال تشترك في الحالية، وتختلف بالخصوصيات التي يتميز بها بعضها عن بعض، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الاختلاف فالحالية زائدة على الخصوصيات، وإنها أي الحالية المشتركة وهي مفهوم الحال حال؛ فتشارك سائر الأحوال في الحالية، وتمتاز عنها قوله : ( بأن ملخص إلخ) فيه إشارة إلى أنها بعينها لا تجري في الأحوال، لأن قيام العرض بالعرض على تقدير وجود ما به الاشتراك وما به الامتياز إنما يلزم إذا كانا ذاتيين لها، وأما إذا كان به الاشتراك عارضا وما به الامتياز نفس ماهياتها فلا وكذا تقوم الموجود بالمعدوم على تقدير عدم أحدهما إنما يلزم إذا كانت الأحوال موجودة، وبالجملة جريان تلك الحجة بخصوصها موقوف على كون المركب موجوداً، وعلى كون ما به الاشتراك وما به الامتياز ذاتيين له، وكلا الأمرين غير متحقق في الحال. ولذا لم يقيد الحقائق بالعرضية وقال : مشتركة في أمور ولم يتمل مركبة من أمور ولم يتعرض لدليل إنهما ليسا بموجودين ولا معدومين إشارة إلى أنه ليس الملحوظ في جريان تلك الحجة في الأحوال خصوصية الأمور المذكورة فيها . قوله : ( وتختلف بالخصوصيات سواء كانتا ذاتيتين أو عرضيتين أو إحداهما عرضية والأخرى ذاتية أو تمام الماهية. قوله: (وإنها حال لاختصاصها بالأحوال، فليست بموجودة؛ لعدم اقتضائها وجود الموصوف، ولا معدومة لاقتضائها ثبوت الموصوف ولظهوره لم يتعرض لبيانه كونها قائمة مع بموجود هو محل الأحوال كأجزاء السواد القائمة بمحله فتدبر فإنه قد خبط فيه بعض الناظرين وقرر النقض بجريان الحجة بعينها متابعة لشارح التجريد وطول الكلام بلا طائل وصاحب المقاصد قرر النقض هكذا الأحوال لو كانت ثابتة لكانت متشاركة في الثبوت متخالفة في الخصوصيات فكان ثبوتها زائداً عليها ضرورة أن ما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز وثبوتها ليس بمنفي فيكون ثابتاً ويتسلسل ولا يخفى أنه على هذا التقرير دليل برأسه وليس نقضاً لتلك الحجة، فالحق ما قاله الشارح. يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يكون اختصاص الذوات بالأحوال كاختصاص حصص الأجناس بالفصول، وحصص الأنواع بالتشخصات .
And as for our view, meaning the deniers of states (nufāt al-aḥwāl): the essences are mutually different in realities, and they share in the necessary concomitants, and that is not impossible, because it is permissible that different realities require one thing necessary for them, unlike the reverse—that essences be common and equal with difference and mutual incompatibility in the concomitants, as is your view—for that is absolutely impossible. And perhaps the deniers of states said: The summary of the argument of the affirmers (muthbitūn) is that realities are common in matters and different by their particularities, and that by which they are common is other than that by which they differ, and they are neither existent nor nonexistent, so the intermediate which is the state (ḥāl) is established. And that is refuted by the fact that states share in stateness (ḥāliyya) and differ by the particularities by which they are distinguished from one another, and that by which they are common is other than that by which they differ, so stateness is additional to the particularities, and that stateness—which is the concept of state (ḥāl)—is itself a state; thus it participates with other states in stateness and is distinguished from them. His statement: 'By summarizing, etc.' — there is an indication that that very argument does not apply to states, because the inherence of an accident in an accident (qiyām al-ʿaraḍ bil-ʿaraḍ) on the assumption of the existence of what is common and what distinguishes only follows if they are essential for it. But if what is common is accidental and what distinguishes is the very quiddities of the states, then it does not follow. Similarly, the inherence of the existent in the nonexistent (qiyām al-mawjūd bil-maʿdūm) on the assumption of the nonexistence of one of them only follows if the states are existent. In sum, the application of that particular argument depends on the composite (murakkab) being existent and on what is common and what distinguishes being essential for it, and both conditions are not realized in the state. Hence he did not restrict realities to being accidental and said: 'common in matters' and did not say 'composed of matters', and he did not address the proof that they are neither existent nor nonexistent—indicating that in the application of that argument to states, the particularity of the mentioned matters is not considered. His statement: 'and they differ by particularities' — whether they are essential or accidental, or one is accidental and the other essential, or the complete quiddity. His statement: 'and it is a state because it is specific to states' — it is not existent because it does not necessitate the existence of the described, nor nonexistent because it necessitates the affirmation (thubūt) of the described. And because it is obvious, he did not address explaining that it subsists with an existent that is the locus of states, like the parts of blackness subsisting in their locus. So reflect, for some thinkers have blundered in this, and they formulated the refutation by applying the very same argument, following the Sharḥ al-Tajrīd, with lengthy useless talk. The author of al-Maqāṣid formulated the refutation thus: 'If states were established, they would share in establishment (thubūt) and differ in particularities, so their establishment would be additional to them, necessarily, because what is common is other than what distinguishes, and their establishment is not negated, so it would be established and so regress follows.' It is not hidden that this formulation is an independent argument, not a refutation of that argument. The truth is what the commentator said. It is said: Why is it not permissible that the distinction of essences by states be like the distinction of the shares of genera by differentiae and the shares of species by individuators?
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage presents the deniers' (nufāt al-aḥwāl) own view and their argument against the proponents of states. First, the deniers assert that, according to their view, essences are inherently different in their realities (ḥaqāʾiq) and can share concomitant attributes without problem; the impossibility is only on the proponents' view where essences are equal but have contradictory concomitants. Next, they summarize the proponents' argument: that real things share in some aspects and differ in others, and since the common and differing aspects are neither existent nor nonexistent, there must be an intermediate 'state'. But the deniers refute this by applying the same reasoning to the states themselves: states share in 'stateness' (ḥāliyya) and differ by particularities, so stateness is additional and would itself be a state, leading to a regress. The commentator then explains that the proponents' argument does not apply to states because the conditions (the common and distinguishing aspects being essential, and the composite being existent) are not met in the case of states. He notes that the author of al-Maqāṣid tried to turn the argument around but ended up producing a separate argument, not a refutation. Finally, a rhetorical question is posed: why can't the distinction by states be analogous to the distinction of shares of genera by differentiae? This suggests the objection is not decisive.

الحقائق

B) Because different realities can necessitate the same thing.
C) Because essences are actually the same.
D) Because attributes are not real.

Key Takeaway
The deniers hold that essences are essentially different yet can share necessary attributes without needing an intermediate state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it mean to focus your self-reflection on your essence (which is hidden from you) rather than its manifestations? This week, spend time in introspection about your 'reality' before Allah, not just your actions.

The Deniers' Refutation of the Proponents' State Argument
The deniers refute the proponents' argument by showing it leads to regress, and the commentator clarifies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the deniers refute the proponents' argument for states by showing that it leads to an infinite regress when applied to states themselves, and the commentator's analysis of why the argument fails.

Core Idea
The same reasoning that proponents use to prove states can be turned against them, but the argument does not apply because the conditions are different.

The deniers then summarize the proponents' key argument: because realities share and differ, there must be an intermediate 'state' that is neither existent nor nonexistent. But the deniers counter by applying the same logic to the states themselves: states share in 'stateness' and differ by particularities, so stateness would be additional—a state of a state, leading to an infinite regress. The commentator explains why this rebuttal is not decisive, as the proponents' argument does not meet the necessary conditions for states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'وربما قال' to the end, along with the translation.
2
Identify the proponents' argument as summarized by the deniers
The proponents argue that since realities share and differ by aspects that are neither existent nor nonexistent, there must be an intermediate state.
3
Understand the deniers' refutation
States themselves share in stateness and differ by particularities, so stateness would be an additional state, leading to a regress.
4
Study the commentator's explanation
The commentator says the deniers' refutation fails because the proponents' argument only works if the common and distinguishing aspects are essential and the composite is existent, which is not the case for states.
5
Reflect on the final analogy
Consider the rhetorical question: why can't states be like differentiae for genera? This suggests the objection is not decisive.

وأما على رأينا يعني نفاة الأحوال : فالذوات متخالفة في الحقائق، وأنها تشترك في اللوازم وذلك غير ممتنع ؛ لجواز أن تكون الحقائق المختلفة مقتضية لأمر واحد لازم لها بخلاف العكس وهو أن تكون الذوات مشتركة متساوية مع الاختلاف والتنافي في اللوازم كما هو رأيكم فإنه ممتنع قطعاً . وربما قال النافون للأحوال : إن ملخص حجة المثبتين لها هو أن الحقائق مشتركة في أمور، ومختلفة بخصوصياتها، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الاختلاف وهما ليسا بموجودين ولا معدومين فقد ثبت الواسطة التي هي الحال، وذلك منقوض بأن الأحوال تشترك في الحالية، وتختلف بالخصوصيات التي يتميز بها بعضها عن بعض، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الاختلاف فالحالية زائدة على الخصوصيات، وإنها أي الحالية المشتركة وهي مفهوم الحال حال؛ فتشارك سائر الأحوال في الحالية، وتمتاز عنها قوله : ( بأن ملخص إلخ) فيه إشارة إلى أنها بعينها لا تجري في الأحوال، لأن قيام العرض بالعرض على تقدير وجود ما به الاشتراك وما به الامتياز إنما يلزم إذا كانا ذاتيين لها، وأما إذا كان به الاشتراك عارضا وما به الامتياز نفس ماهياتها فلا وكذا تقوم الموجود بالمعدوم على تقدير عدم أحدهما إنما يلزم إذا كانت الأحوال موجودة، وبالجملة جريان تلك الحجة بخصوصها موقوف على كون المركب موجوداً، وعلى كون ما به الاشتراك وما به الامتياز ذاتيين له، وكلا الأمرين غير متحقق في الحال. ولذا لم يقيد الحقائق بالعرضية وقال : مشتركة في أمور ولم يتمل مركبة من أمور ولم يتعرض لدليل إنهما ليسا بموجودين ولا معدومين إشارة إلى أنه ليس الملحوظ في جريان تلك الحجة في الأحوال خصوصية الأمور المذكورة فيها . قوله : ( وتختلف بالخصوصيات سواء كانتا ذاتيتين أو عرضيتين أو إحداهما عرضية والأخرى ذاتية أو تمام الماهية. قوله: (وإنها حال لاختصاصها بالأحوال، فليست بموجودة؛ لعدم اقتضائها وجود الموصوف، ولا معدومة لاقتضائها ثبوت الموصوف ولظهوره لم يتعرض لبيانه كونها قائمة مع بموجود هو محل الأحوال كأجزاء السواد القائمة بمحله فتدبر فإنه قد خبط فيه بعض الناظرين وقرر النقض بجريان الحجة بعينها متابعة لشارح التجريد وطول الكلام بلا طائل وصاحب المقاصد قرر النقض هكذا الأحوال لو كانت ثابتة لكانت متشاركة في الثبوت متخالفة في الخصوصيات فكان ثبوتها زائداً عليها ضرورة أن ما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز وثبوتها ليس بمنفي فيكون ثابتاً ويتسلسل ولا يخفى أنه على هذا التقرير دليل برأسه وليس نقضاً لتلك الحجة، فالحق ما قاله الشارح. يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يكون اختصاص الذوات بالأحوال كاختصاص حصص الأجناس بالفصول، وحصص الأنواع بالتشخصات .
And as for our view, meaning the deniers of states (nufāt al-aḥwāl): the essences are mutually different in realities, and they share in the necessary concomitants, and that is not impossible, because it is permissible that different realities require one thing necessary for them, unlike the reverse—that essences be common and equal with difference and mutual incompatibility in the concomitants, as is your view—for that is absolutely impossible. And perhaps the deniers of states said: The summary of the argument of the affirmers (muthbitūn) is that realities are common in matters and different by their particularities, and that by which they are common is other than that by which they differ, and they are neither existent nor nonexistent, so the intermediate which is the state (ḥāl) is established. And that is refuted by the fact that states share in stateness (ḥāliyya) and differ by the particularities by which they are distinguished from one another, and that by which they are common is other than that by which they differ, so stateness is additional to the particularities, and that stateness—which is the concept of state (ḥāl)—is itself a state; thus it participates with other states in stateness and is distinguished from them. His statement: 'By summarizing, etc.' — there is an indication that that very argument does not apply to states, because the inherence of an accident in an accident (qiyām al-ʿaraḍ bil-ʿaraḍ) on the assumption of the existence of what is common and what distinguishes only follows if they are essential for it. But if what is common is accidental and what distinguishes is the very quiddities of the states, then it does not follow. Similarly, the inherence of the existent in the nonexistent (qiyām al-mawjūd bil-maʿdūm) on the assumption of the nonexistence of one of them only follows if the states are existent. In sum, the application of that particular argument depends on the composite (murakkab) being existent and on what is common and what distinguishes being essential for it, and both conditions are not realized in the state. Hence he did not restrict realities to being accidental and said: 'common in matters' and did not say 'composed of matters', and he did not address the proof that they are neither existent nor nonexistent—indicating that in the application of that argument to states, the particularity of the mentioned matters is not considered. His statement: 'and they differ by particularities' — whether they are essential or accidental, or one is accidental and the other essential, or the complete quiddity. His statement: 'and it is a state because it is specific to states' — it is not existent because it does not necessitate the existence of the described, nor nonexistent because it necessitates the affirmation (thubūt) of the described. And because it is obvious, he did not address explaining that it subsists with an existent that is the locus of states, like the parts of blackness subsisting in their locus. So reflect, for some thinkers have blundered in this, and they formulated the refutation by applying the very same argument, following the Sharḥ al-Tajrīd, with lengthy useless talk. The author of al-Maqāṣid formulated the refutation thus: 'If states were established, they would share in establishment (thubūt) and differ in particularities, so their establishment would be additional to them, necessarily, because what is common is other than what distinguishes, and their establishment is not negated, so it would be established and so regress follows.' It is not hidden that this formulation is an independent argument, not a refutation of that argument. The truth is what the commentator said. It is said: Why is it not permissible that the distinction of essences by states be like the distinction of the shares of genera by differentiae and the shares of species by individuators?

Commentary
This passage presents the deniers' (nufāt al-aḥwāl) own view and their argument against the proponents of states. First, the deniers assert that, according to their view, essences are inherently different in their realities (ḥaqāʾiq) and can share concomitant attributes without problem; the impossibility is only on the proponents' view where essences are equal but have contradictory concomitants. The Deniers' Argument on Essences and Concomitants
The deniers of states argue that essences are diverse and share only in necessary concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the deniers of states maintain that essences are diverse in their realities and only share in necessary concomitants, and how they argue against the proponents of states.

Core Idea
Essences are diverse; only their attributes are shared.

This lesson examines the position of the Deniers of States (Nufat al-Ahwal) on the nature of essences and their attributes. They argue that essences (dhawat) are fundamentally diverse in their realities, and they only share in the necessary concomitants (lawazim) that follow from them. This view contrasts with that of the proponents of states, who claim essences are identical and distinguished by states. The passage presents their core argument and a justification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the debate
The issue is whether essences are identical and distinguished by states, or diverse and share only in concomitants. Recognize the two opposing views.
This debate is central to the theory of states in kalam.
2
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the gist of the argument.
3
Identify the two parts
Notice the rhetorical question challenging the proponents' view, followed by the deniers' own position with a justification.
The passage moves from critique to positive statement.
4
Examine the justification
Understand why different realities can necessitate a single concomitant, and why the opposite is impossible.
The key is the asymmetry: diverse realities can concur on one attribute, but identical realities cannot have contradictory attributes.
5
Reflect on implications
Consider how this argument supports the deniers' overall rejection of states as real entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكيف يتصور الاشتراك والتساوي في الحقيقة مع الامتياز باللوازم التي هي الأحوال؟ وأما على رأينا يعني نفاة الأحوال: فالذوات متخالفة في الحقائق، وأنها تشترك في اللوازم وذلك غير ممتنع؛ لجواز أن تكون الحقائق المختلفة مقتضية لأمر واحد لازم لها بخلاف العكس) وهو أن تكون الذوات مشتركة متساوية مع الاختلاف والتنافي في اللوازم كما هو رأيكم فإنه ممتنع قطعاً.
How can there be conceived participation and equality in the reality together with distinction through the necessary concomitants which are the states? And according to our view—meaning the deniers of states—the essences are diverse in the realities, and they share in the necessary concomitants, and that is not impossible; because it is possible that the different realities necessitate one thing that is necessary for them, contrary to the opposite—which is that the essences be shared and equal with difference and mutual exclusion in the necessary concomitants, as is your view, for it is absolutely impossible.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage presents the position of those who deny the metaphysical reality of states (ahwal). It begins with a rhetorical question: How can there be participation and equality in the essence while there is distinction by states (which are the necessary concomitants)? The implication is that this is problematic. Then it states the deniers' own view: essences (dhawat) are diverse in their realities, and they only share in the necessary concomitants (lawazim) that follow their essences, and this is not impossible. The justification is that different realities can necessitate a single necessary concomitant for each, whereas the opposite—that essences are shared and equal while contradicting each other in their concomitants—is absolutely impossible. This is directed against the proponents of states who maintain that essences are identical in reality but distinguished by the states, a view the deniers consider untenable.

Correction: They actually assert that essences are diverse in realities and only share in necessary concomitants.

C) Both essences and states are identical.

Key Takeaway

If you cannot name it, are you mistaking your passing states for your essence? Take a moment to write down that unchanging reality and commit to living from it.
