The Objection of Infinite Regress of States and Its Answer
The deniers object that the argument for states leads to infinite regress; two answers are given and discussed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the deniers of states use the proponents' own logic to argue for infinite regress, and the two main responses to this objection.

Core Idea
The same logic that proves states can be turned against them, but defenders offer two responses.

This passage presents a sophisticated dialectical exchange. The deniers of states attempt to refute the proponents by showing that the very reasoning used to establish states—that shared and distinguishing features are neither existent nor non-existent—applies to states themselves, generating an infinite chain. The proponents offer two responses: accepting the regress (which is contested) or identifying one element as existent to halt the regress. This passage is a masterclass in kalam reasoning about the nature of states and the limits of conceptual analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand how the deniers apply the proponents' argument to states themselves, leading to infinite regress.
Pay attention to the two formulations: one via common 'state-ness' and one via particularities.
2
Identify the two answers
The first answer accepts the regress; the second answer selects a specific element as existent to break the chain.
The first answer is presented briefly, the second is developed extensively.
3
Analyze the strengths and weaknesses
Consider Imam al-Razi's critique of the first answer, and the detailed discussion of the second answer regarding constitutive elements and subsistence.
The second answer involves subtle distinctions about essential vs. accidental concepts.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Appreciate the dialectical sophistication and the importance of precise definitions in kalam.
This passage shows how objections can be met through careful conceptual analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما قال النافون للأحوال : إن ملخص حجة المثبتين لها هو أن الحقائق مشتركة في أمور، ومختلفة بخصوصياتها، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الاختلاف وهما ليسا بموجودين ولا معدومين فثبت الواسطة التي هي الحال، وذلك منقوض بأن الأحوال تشترك في الحالية، وتختلف بالخصوصيات التي يتميز بها بعضها عن بعض، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الاختلاف فالحالية زائدة على الخصوصيات، وإنها أي الحالية المشتركة وهي مفهوم الحال حال؛ فتشارك سائر الأحوال في الحالية، وتمتاز عنها بخصوصية وليس شيء من المشترك والمميز موجوداً ولا معدوماً، فثبت حال آخر، فتتسلسل الأحوال إلى غير النهاية. أو نقول وإنها أي كل واحدة من الخصوصيات حال تشارك سائر الأحوال في مفهوم الحال وتمتاز عنها بخصوصية أخرى، وهكذا . وأجيب عنه بوجهين : الأول : التزام التسلسل في الأحوال، وورده الإمام الرازي بأنه يسد باب إثبات الصانع. وفيه نظر؛ لأن إثبات الصانع إنما يتوقف قوله : ( وليس شيء إلخ ) لما مر بعينه . قوله : ( أو تقول إلخ ) يعني يجوز أن يكون ضمير إنها راجعة إلى الخصوصيات . قوله : ( وليس شيء من المشترك والمميز موجوداً ولا معدوماً فثبت حال آخر لأنهما وصفان قائمان بما يقوم به الحال أعني الموجود؛ لأن مقوم الشيء يقوم بما يقوم به الشيء، فاندفع اعتراض الأبهري بعدم لزوم حال آخر بناء على عدم القيام بالموجود. قوله : ( أو نقول وإنها إلخ ) فيه بحث : لأن النقض بأي الوجهين قرر إنما يتم إذا كان مفهوم الحال ذاتياً لما تحته من الخصوصيات حتى يلزم تمايزها بفصول هي أحوال أيضاً مشتركة في مفهوم مطلق الحال، ويلزم التسلسل وهو ممنوع؛ لجواز أن يكون عرضاً عاماً لها، ويكون تمايزها بذواتها، فلا يلزم التسلسل وبالجملة مبنى الوجه الثاني لمثبتي الأحوال أن يكون ما به الاشتراك والامتياز من مقومات الحقائق الموجودة وذاتياتها كما صرحوا به، وإلا فلا محذور كونهما معدومين فلا يرد النقض إلا بعد إثبات كون كل من المميز والمشترك ذاتياً للأحوال . فإن قلت : لو سلم أنهما ذاتيان لها لم يتوجه النقض أيضاً لجواز أن يكون أحدهما أو كلاهما عدمياً، ولا يلزم تقوم الموجود بالمعدوم، بل تقوم ما ليس بمعدوم ولا موجود بالمعدوم، ولا نسلم استحالته، فإن الحال لما كانت واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم فلها حظ من الطرفين، فإنهم يجعلونه قد تجاوز في التحقق حد العدم ولم يبلغ حد الوجود، ولذلك جوزوا أن يكون الحال مقوماً للحقائق الموجودة ولم يجوزوا أن يكون المعدوم مقوماً لها، فلا عليهم أن يجوزوا تقوم الحال بالمعدوم . قلت : كلامنا في الأحوال التي أثبتوها للحقائق الفرضية الموجودة مقومات لها، ولا يجوز تقومها بالمعدوم، وإلا لزم تقوم تلك الحقائق به لأن مقوم المقوم مقوم . وقد يجاب عن النقض باختيار أن الأمر المشترك وهو مفهوم الحال حال والأمر المختص موجود، فلا يلزم قيام العرض بالعرض ولا التقوم بالمعدوم. ولا يمكن نقل الكلام إلى مفهوم الحال؛ لأنه مشترك بين نفسه والأحوال الخاصة فلا يكون لمفهوم الحال حال زائد على نفسه حتى يتسلسل، كما سيذكره الشارح. فإن قلت : يتم النقض في الأحوال القائمة بالأعراض إذ لو كان إحدى مقوماتها موجودة لزم قيام العرض بالعرض؛ إذ لا شك أن مقوم الشيء يقوم بما يقوم به ذلك الشيء كما مر . قلت : إن كان الكلام في الأحوال المقومة للأعراض فليست بقائمة بها، بل بمحالها، وإن كان في الأحوال الخارجة القائمة بها فقد عرفت أن الاستدلال لا يتم بجواز تقوم الحال بالمعدوم فتأمل. قوله : ( وفيه نظر إلخ ) رده الشارح في حواشي التجريد بما حاصله : أن برهان التطبيق يدل
And perhaps the deniers of states said: The summary of the argument of the affirmers of states is that truths share in matters and differ in their particularities, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ, and both are neither existent nor non-existent, so the intermediary which is the state is established. That is refuted by the fact that states share in 'state-ness' (al-haliyya) and differ by the particularities through which some are distinguished from others, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ, so state-ness is additional to the particularities, and that common state-ness, which is the concept of state, is a state; so it participates with other states in state-ness and is distinguished from them by a particularity, and nothing of the shared and the distinguishing is existent nor non-existent, so another state is established; thus states regress to infinity. Or we say: that each one of the particularities is a state that participates with other states in the concept of state and is distinguished from them by another particularity, and so on. And it was answered by two ways: First: acceptance of the regress in states, and Imam al-Razi refuted it by saying it blocks the door of proving the Maker. But there is consideration in that; because proving the Maker depends only on... [then commentary continues]. His statement: 'and nothing... etc.' refers to what has passed. His statement: 'or you say... etc.' means that it is possible that the pronoun 'innaha' refers to the particularities. His statement: 'and nothing of the shared and the distinguishing is existent nor non-existent, so another state is established' because they are two attributes subsisting in that by which the state subsists, namely the existent; because the constitutive element of a thing subsists in that by which the thing subsists, so the objection of al-Abhari that no other state is necessary is repelled based on the lack of subsistence in the existent. His statement: 'or we say that they etc.' has a discussion: because the refutation in whichever of the two ways it is formulated is only valid if the concept of state is essential to the particularities under it, so that their differentiation is by differentiae which are also states sharing in the absolute concept of state, and regression follows, which is prevented; because it is possible that it is a general accident for them, and their differentiation is by their essences, so regression does not follow. In summary, the basis of the second way for the affirmers of states is that that by which sharing and distinction are [must be] from the constitutive elements of the existing truths and their essential properties as they stated, otherwise there is no problem in their being non-existent, so the refutation is only valid after proving that each of the distinguishing and the shared is essential to the states. If you say: if it is admitted that they are essential for them, the refutation still does not apply because it is possible that one or both are non-existential, and the subsistence of the existent by the non-existent is not required; rather the subsistence of that which is neither non-existent nor existent by the non-existent, and we do not admit its impossibility. For the state, since it is intermediate between existent and non-existent, has a share from both sides; they consider that it has exceeded the limit of non-existence in realization but has not reached the limit of existence, therefore they permitted that the state be constitutive of existing truths and they did not permit that the non-existent be constitutive of them, so they are not obliged to permit the subsistence of the state by the non-existent. I say: our discussion is about the states which they established for hypothetical existing truths as constitutive of them, and their subsistence by the non-existent is not permissible, otherwise the subsistence of those truths by it would follow because the constitutive of the constitutive is constitutive. And it may be answered about the refutation by choosing that the common matter, which is the concept of state, is a state and the specific matter is existent, so neither the subsistence of an accident by an accident nor subsistence by the non-existent follows. And it is not possible to transfer the discussion to the concept of state because it is common between itself and the particular states, so the concept of state does not have a state additional to itself such that it would regress, as the commentator will mention. If you say: the refutation is valid regarding states that subsist in accidents, because if one of their constitutive elements were existent, the subsistence of an accident by an accident would follow, since there is no doubt that the constitutive element of a thing subsists in that by which the thing subsists, as previously. I say: if the discussion is about states that are constitutive of accidents, they do not subsist in them but rather in their subjects; and if it is about the external states that subsist in them, you already know that the argument is not valid due to the possibility of the subsistence of the state by the non-existent. So reflect. His statement: 'and there is consideration in that etc.' is refuted by the commentator in his glosses on al-Tajrid by saying, in summary, that the proof of correspondence indicates...
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage presents a complex dialectical exchange regarding the doctrine of states (ahwal). It begins with the deniers' objection (naqd) against the proponents (muthbitun) of states. The proponents argue that truths share in some aspects and differ in particularities; that by which they share is not the same as that by which they differ, and both are neither existent nor non-existent, hence the intermediary, which is the state, is established. The deniers counter that this argument applies equally to states themselves: states share in 'state-ness' (al-haliyya) and differ by particularities; the shared state-ness is itself a state, leading to another state, ad infinitum. Alternatively, each particularity is a state sharing in the concept of state and distinguished by another particularity, again leading to infinite regress. The answer to this objection is presented in two ways. The first is to accept the regress (iltizam al-tasalsul) in the states, but Imam al-Razi refutes this acceptance because it blocks the proof of the Creator (since that proof relies on the finitude of regress). The commentator notes that this refutation is debatable. The commentator then provides extensive analysis, discussing various subtleties such as whether the common concept is essential to the particularities, whether the shared and distinguishing elements are constitutive of the truths, and whether states can subsist in non-existent entities. He also offers an alternative answer to the objection: the common concept of state is itself a state, but the specific particularity is existent, thus avoiding both accident-on-accident subsistence and subsistence by non-existent. Further hypothetical objections and responses are considered, including the case of states that subsist in accidents. The commentary concludes with a reference to the commentator's gloss on al-Tajrid. This passage is a sophisticated exercise in kalam dialectic, defending the doctrine of states against a charge of infinite regress.

الحالية
التسلسل
المثبتين
النافون
الحال
قيام العرض بالعرض
مقوم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the infinite regress objection is fatal to the doctrine of states.
Correction: The text shows multiple responses that defend the doctrine, especially the second answer that stops the regress by positing one element as existent.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection presented by the deniers of states against the proponents of states?
A) The proponents' argument requires that states be both existent and non-existent simultaneously.
B) The proponents' argument leads to an infinite regress of states because the shared 'state-ness' is itself a state.
C) The proponents cannot prove the existence of states at all.
D) The proponents' argument contradicts the attributes of God.

Key Takeaway
The objection of infinite regress is a serious challenge to the doctrine of states, but the proponents have two responses, the second of which avoids regress by positing that the specific particularity is existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have any core beliefs—religious or otherwise—that, if you pushed them as far as the deniers did here, would lead to an infinite regress you cannot resolve? Would you accept the regress or try to block it, and what would that cost you?
?
This lesson shows that even the most careful theologians disagree on whether a response is sufficient. When you encounter a deep theological objection that seems unanswerable, is your first instinct to defend your position at all costs, or to honestly reconsider its foundations? Which response is more pleasing to Allah?
?
The commentator notes that al-Razi's objection 'is debatable.' How comfortable are you with the idea that even the greatest scholars can be challenged on logical grounds? Does that unsettle your trust in scholarship, or does it deepen your humility before the truth?

The Objection That States Are Not Similar or Different
Second objection against states not being similar or different, with response and consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the theory of states (ahwal) and how Imam al-Razi responds by showing that similarity and difference are unavoidable for any two mental conceptions, along with a critical consideration from the commentator.

Core Idea
Similarity and difference are unavoidable for any two mental concepts, even states.

This lesson examines a powerful objection against the theory of states (ahwal): that states cannot be described as similar or different without contradiction. Imam al-Razi provides a crisp logical response, asserting that any two mental concepts must be either identical or distinct, implying similarity or difference. The commentator then offers a further consideration about the ontological status of similarity and difference themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage's first part, which states that states cannot be described as similar (by sharing statehood) or different (by particularities). Understand why this is problematic for the theory.
The objection claims that describing states as similar or different would require them to be existents, which they are not.
2
Study Imam al-Razi's response
Imam al-Razi argues that the objection is based on ignorance: any two mental concepts must be either identical in conception (similar) or not (different). Thus, states must be one or the other.
This response shows that similarity and difference are not limited to existents.
3
Examine the critical consideration
The commentator notes that the objectors themselves treat similarity and difference either as existent attributes or as states, but in both cases they subsist only in the existent, which may imply a deeper problem.
This consideration hints at a debate about the ontological status of relations.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this exchange strengthens or weakens the theory of states. Does al-Razi's response fully resolve the objection? Why does the commentator add a critical note?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والثاني: أن الأحوال لا توصف بالتماثل والاختلاف)، فلا يصح أن يقال : إنها مشتركة في الحالية؛ لأنه وصف لها بالتماثل ولا أنها متمايزة بخصوصياتها؛ لأنه وصف لها بالاختلاف . ( وأجاب ) الإمام الرازي ( عنه ( أيضا بأن ذلك جهالة ؛ لأن كل أمرين يشير إليهما العقل بوجه من الوجوه إما أن يكون المتصور من أحدهما هو المتصور من الآخر أولا، فعلى الأول بينهما تماثل، وعلى الثاني اختلاف فلا مخرج عنهما . ( وفيه نظر لأنهم جعلوا التماثل والاختلاف : إما صفة موجودة ( أو حالاً وعلى كلا التقديرين لا يقوم إلا بالموجود
(And the second: that states are not described as similarity and difference), so it is not correct to say that they share in statehood because that would be describing them as similar, nor that they are distinguished by their particularities because that would be describing them as different. (And Imam al-Razi answered) it also (that this is ignorance; for every two things that the mind points to in any way, either the conception of one is the conception of the other or not. On the first, there is similarity between them; on the second, difference, so there is no escape from them). (And there is a consideration in it, because they made similarity and difference either an existent attribute or a state, and in both estimations they subsist only in the existent.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against ahwal: that states cannot be described as similar or different. The objection states that calling them 'similar' in being states or 'different' by particularities would require them to be described by similarity and difference, which is inappropriate for states. Imam al-Razi responds that this is ignorance—any two mental concepts are either identical (similar) or not (different), so there is no escape; all states must be either similar or different. The commentator (al-Sharh) then notes a consideration: the objectors' use of 'similarity' and 'difference' presupposes them to be either existent attributes or states, and in both cases they subsist only in the existent. This hints at a deeper debate about the ontological status of these relations.

Key Terms

اختلاف (ikhtilaf)
حالية (haliyya)
جهالة (jahala)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Similarity and difference can only be predicated of existent entities, not of non-existent mental constructs like states.
Correction: Imam al-Razi demonstrates that for any two mental concepts, the conception itself is either identical or not, which is a logical relation independent of existence. Therefore, states can indeed be described as similar or different in conception.

Checkpoint
What is Imam al-Razi's main response to the objection that states cannot be described as similar or different?
A) He agrees and argues that states are beyond similarity and difference.
B) He states that any two mental concepts must be either identical in conception (similar) or not (different), so similarity and difference are unavoidable.
C) He claims that similarity and difference are accidental qualities that only exist in the mind.
D) He suggests that the objection is valid but irrelevant to the theory of states.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi shows that similarity and difference are inevitable logical relations between any two mental concepts, thereby defending the possibility of describing states with these terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you categorize two of your own ideas or beliefs as 'similar' or 'different', do you treat those labels as fixed truths? How does al-Razi's argument that similarity and difference are inescapable mental comparisons challenge your tendency to reify such categories? Will you now be more cautious before declaring two things as 'the same' or 'completely different'?
?
Think of a belief you hold that you consider uniquely yours. Now apply al-Razi's principle: with respect to that belief, is your conception of it identical to someone else's or different? What does the inevitability of this comparison teach you about humility regarding your own intellectual originality? How will you respond when you realize that even your most personal ideas are inevitably comparable to others'?

Glosses on the First and Second Objections: Regress, States, and Similarity
Supplementary commentary clarifying the proponents' acceptance of regress, the nature of relations, and the use of similarity and difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key clarifications regarding the acceptance of regress in states, the distinction between states and intellectual relations, and the interpretation of similarity and difference in the debate over the two objections.

Core Idea
States are fixed realities; regress in them does not harm the proof of creation.

These glosses provide crucial supplementary insights into the first and second objections of the debate. They address how proponents accept regress in states without undermining the proof of the Creator, distinguish states from relations and negations that depend on mental consideration, and clarify the meaning of similarity and difference in the second objection. By studying these glosses, you will see how the commentator defends the consistency of the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the commentator's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repeated paragraph – it is a restatement of the first gloss.
2
Identify the three main clarifications
The passage contains three distinct glosses: (1) acceptance of regress in states does not block the proof of the Creator, (2) states are fixed in themselves unlike relations/negations, (3) similarity and difference are used in a linguistic sense in the second objection.
3
Reflect on the significance
Think about how each clarification defends the proof of creation and the coherence of the Ash'ari doctrine of states. Why does the commentator insist that states are not merely mental considerations?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والتزامهم إلخ) يعني التزامهم التسلسل في الأحوال لا ينافي امتناعه في الأمور الموجودة، وما قاله الشارح في حواشي شرح التجريد من أن برهان التطبيق يدل على امتناع ترتيب أمور غير متناهية مجتمعة في الثبوت، سواء كانت موجودات أو أحوالاً، وهذا البرهان هو في إبطال حوادث لا أول لها، وإثبات الصانع فمراد الإمام أن تجويز التسلسل في الأحوال يسد باب إثبات الصانع بالطريق الذي اعتمدوا عليه فمدفوع بأن قولهم بالمعدومات الثابتة الغير المتناهية مع جريان التطبيق فيها، إذ الترتب ليس بشرط فيه عندهم يوجب سد باب إثبات الصانع، بناء على اشتراط الوجود في جريانه، فكيف التزام التسلسل في الأحوال يوجب ذلك . قوله : (كما لا يمتنع إلخ الأولى تركه ؛ إذ الإضافات والسلوب وجودها بحسب اعتبار العقل، فإذا اعتبرها تسلسلت، وإذا لم يعتبرها انقطعت، بخلاف الأحوال لأنها ثابتة في أنفسها، وليس ثبوتها باعتبار العقل . قوله : ( بينهما تماثل أي في ذلك المتصور . فلا مخرج عنهما إذ لا واسطة بين النقيضين. قوله : ( لأنهم جعلوا إلخ) منع صاحب المقاصد هذا الجعل فلا بد له من شاهد من قوله : (موجودة) قيد بذلك لأن الصفة المعدومة تقوم بالمعدوم . كلامهم . على امتناع ترتيب أمور غير متناهية مجتمعة في الثبوت سواء كانت موجودات أو أحوالاً. وهذا البرهان هو المعتمد عندهم في إبطال حوادث لا أول لها وإثبات الصانع، فمراد الإمام أن تجويز التسلسل في الأحوال يسد باب إثبات الصانع بالطريق الذي سلكوه واعتمدوا عليه، وهذا القدر يكفي إلزاما لهم . قوله : ( لأنه وصف لها بالتماثل حمل التماثل على معناه الاصطلاحي حتى يتوقف على كون الحالية من أخص الصفات النفسية وهو محل بحث وحمله على معناه اللغوي لا يتوقف عليه، لكن في كونه من الأحوال تردد وبالجملة مراد الناقض بالاشتراك والاختلاف معناهما اللغويان، والأحوال بل المعدومات أيضاً توصف بهما، فجواب الإمام حق ولا يرد نظر المصنف .
His statement: (And their acceptance etc.) means their acceptance of regress in states does not contradict its impossibility in existing things, and what the commentator said in the glosses of the commentary on al-Tajrid that the proof of application indicates the impossibility of an ordered series of infinite things united in subsistence, whether they be existents or states, and this proof is used to refute beginningless events and to prove the Maker; so the intent of the Imam is that allowing regress in states blocks the door of proving the Maker by the path they relied on, but it is refuted by their saying about the non-existent, non-finite things with the application occurring in them, since order is not a condition for it according to them, would block the door of proving the Maker, based on the condition of existence in its operation; so how does accepting regress in states necessitate that? His statement: (Just as it is not impossible etc.) – it would have been better to omit it; for relations and negations have existence according to the consideration of the intellect; if the intellect considers them, they regress; if it does not consider them, they terminate, unlike states because they are established in themselves, and their subsistence is not by consideration of the intellect. His statement: (Between them is similarity) i.e., in that conceived. So there is no escape from them since there is no intermediate between contradictories. His statement: (Because they made etc.) – the author of al-Maqasid denied this making, so he must have evidence from his statement: (existent) restricted thereby because the non-existent attribute subsists in the non-existent. Their statement. On the impossibility of an ordered series of infinite things united in subsistence, whether they be existents or states. And this proof is the one relied upon by them in refuting beginningless events and proving the Maker, so the intent of the Imam is that allowing regress in states blocks the door of proving the Maker by the path they followed and relied upon, and this suffices as an obligation upon them. His statement: (Because describing them by similarity) – similarity is taken in its technical meaning so that it depends on statehood being among the most specific of the essential attributes, which is a matter of investigation, and taking it in its linguistic meaning does not depend on that, but there is hesitation about whether it is among the states. In summary, the objector by 'sharing' and 'difference' means their linguistic meanings, and states, even non-existents, are described by them, so the answer of the Imam is correct and the author's consideration does not apply.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a set of glosses that further clarify the arguments from the first two objections. The first gloss explains that the proponents' acceptance of regress in states does not contradict its impossibility in existents, and it discusses an objection from Imam al-Razi about blocking the proof of the Creator. It argues that the proof of application does not require existence, so accepting regress in non-existent states does not block the proof. The second gloss criticizes the comparison between states and relations/negations, stating that relations and negations depend on intellectual consideration, whereas states are fixed in themselves. The third gloss clarifies that 'similarity' in the second objection refers to the conceived, and there is no middle between similarity and difference. The fourth gloss discusses the technical vs. linguistic meaning of similarity and difference, concluding that the objector intended the linguistic meaning, and thus states (and even non-existents) can be described as similar or different, making Imam al-Razi's answer correct and overriding the author's objection.

إضافات (idafat)
سلوب (sulub)
تماثل (tamathul)
اختلاف (ikhtilaf)
حالية (haliyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq) requires actual existence in the series.
Correction: The gloss clarifies that for proponents of non-existent things, the proof does not require existence because order is not a condition for it; thus regress in states does not block the proof.

Checkpoint
According to the gloss, why does the comparison between states and relations/negations (idafat wa sulub) not hold?
A) Because relations and negations are existent things, while states are not.
B) Because relations and negations have existence only by intellectual consideration, while states are fixed in themselves.
C) Because relations and negations do not regress, while states do.
D) Because the proof of application does not apply to relations and negations.

Key Takeaway
The glosses reinforce that states are real, regress in them is permissible without undermining the proof of creation, and similarity/difference are to be understood linguistically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the first gloss, you learned that regress in states does not block the proof of the Creator. Have you ever allowed a subtle doubt about infinite regress to shake your conviction in Allah's existence? Pledge to study the proof of application thoroughly so that such doubts no longer unsettle you.
?
The second gloss contrasts states (fixed realities) with relations/negations (mind-dependent). Are you inclined to treat worldly relations—status, wealth, opinions—as if they are permanent realities? List one relation you consider important and consciously acknowledge its mental dependence today.
?
The third gloss clarifies that similarity and difference are understood linguistically, not technically, in this context. How often do you argue over terminology while missing the intended meaning? In your next discussion about religion, focus on the core message rather than getting caught up in technicalities, as the gloss teaches.

The Nature of States (Ahwal)
Al-Jurjani and al-Razi define the ontological status of states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari concept of a 'state' (hal) as something that is neither existent nor non-existent, and how its concept is considered a negation (salb).

Core Idea
A state is not a thing; it is a mental construct defined by negation.

The notion of 'states' (ahwal) is pivotal in Ash'ari theology for explaining attributes that are neither essences nor separate entities. This passage clarifies that states are not existent, and even the concept of a state is itself a negation, avoiding the pitfalls of reification.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the phrase 'الأحوال التي ليست بموجودة' and al-Razi's statement that the concept of a state is a negation.
Focus on the negation: 'سلب'.
2
Understand 'neither existent nor non-existent'
Reflect on the meaning of 'ليس موجوداً ولا معدوماً'. Why is this considered a negation?
This distinguishes states from both existents and absolute non-existents.
3
Connect to the broader kalam debate
Consider how this definition prevents infinite regress in the theory of states.
Al-Razi's answer aims to dissolve the problem.

( وأجاب ) الإمام الرازي ( عنه ( أيضا بأن ذلك جهالة ؛ لأن كل أمرين يشير إليهما العقل بوجه من الوجوه إما أن يكون المتصور من أحدهما هو المتصور من الآخر أولا، فعلى الأول بينهما تماثل، وعلى الثاني اختلاف فلا مخرج عنهما . ( وفيه نظر لأنهم جعلوا التماثل والاختلاف : إما صفة موجودة ( أو حالاً وعلى كلا التقديرين لا يقوم إلا بالموجود، أما على الأول فلان وجود الصفة فرع وجود الموصوف، وأما على المعتمد قوله : ( والتزامهم إلخ) يعني التزامهم التسلسل في الأحوال لا ينافي امتناعه في الأمور الموجودة، وما قاله الشارح في حواشي شرح التجريد من أن برهان التطبيق يدل على امتناع ترتب أمور غير متناهية مجتمعة في الثبوت، سواء كانت موجودات المتناهية مع جريان التطبيق فيها، إذ الترتب ليس بشرط فيه عندهم يوجب سد باب إثبات الصانع، بناء على اشتراط الوجود في جريانه، فكيف التزام التسلسل في الأحوال يوجب ذلك . قوله : (كما لا يمتنع إلخ الأولى تركه قوله فلا مخرج عنهما إذ لا واسطة بين النقيضين. قوله : ( لأنهم جعلوا إلخ) منع صاحب المقاصد هذا الجعل فلا بد له من شاهد من قوله : (موجودة) قيد بذلك لأن الصفة المعدومة تقوم بالمعدوم . كلامهم . على امتناع ترتيب أمور غير متناهية مجتمعة في الثبوت سواء كانت موجودات أو أحوالاً. وهذا البرهان هو المعتمد عندهم في إبطال حوادث لا أول لها وإثبات الصانع، فمراد الإمام أن تجويز التسلسل في الأحوال يسد باب إثبات الصانع بالطريق الذي سلكوه واعتمدوا عليه، وهذا القدر يكفي إلزاما لهم . قوله : ( لأنه وصف لها بالتماثل حمل التماثل على معناه الاصطلاحي حتى يتوقف على كون الحالية من أخص الصفات النفسية وهو محل بحث وحمله على معناه اللغوي لا يتوقف عليه، لكن في كونه من الأحوال تردد وبالجملة مراد الناقض بالاشتراك والاختلاف معناهما اللغويان، والأحوال بل المعدومات أيضاً توصف بهما، فجواب الإمام حق ولا يرد نظر المصنف . الثاني فلأن الحال لا يقوم إلا بالموجود ) فإطلاقهما ) أي إطلاق التماثل والاختلاف على الأحوال يكون بمعنى آخر؛ فلا يكون الحكم بأن الأحوال لا توصف بهما بالمعنى الأول جهالة . ثم إن الإمام الرازي بعد ما زيف الوجهين المذكورين في الجواب (أجاب) عن كلام النافين ( بأن الحال أي مفهومه (ليس حالاً، بل هو سلب إذ معناه كونه ليس موجوداً ولا معدوماً وكل مفهوم اعتبر فيه سلب كان معدوماً لا حالا . وهذا الجواب إنما يتمشى إذا ادعي أن مفهوم الحال حال، وحينئذ يجاب بجواب آخر أيضاً ، وهو أن مفهوم الحال مشترك بين نفسه والأحوال الخاصة، فلا يكون لمفهوم الحال حال زائد على نفسه حتى يتسلسل وأما إذا ادعي أن الخصوصيات المميزة لبعض الأحوال عن بعض أحوال أيضاً، فلا يتم ذلك الجواب إلا إذا قيل : إن الخصوصيات أيضا سلوب. واعلم أن المباحث المتعلقة بثبوت المعدوم والحال أحكام فاسدة مبنية على أصول باطلة فلذلك أعرضنا عن الإطناب فيها وتضييع الأوقات في توجيهاتها .
First Observatory - Seventh Purpose: Conclusion. On the impossibility of infinite regress in existing things, and their commitment [to it] does not contradict this impossibility; because it is possible that infinite regress is impossible in existents but not impossible in states (ahwal) which are not existent, just as it is not impossible in relations (idafat) and privations (sulub) by agreement. And the second: that states are not described by similarity and difference, so it is not correct to say that they share in being states (haliyya), because that is describing them with similarity, nor that they are distinguished by their particularities, because that is describing them with difference. And Imam al-Razi answered him also by saying that this is ignorance; because any two things that the mind points to in some respect: either the conception of one is the conception of the other or not. On the first, there is similarity between them; on the second, there is difference; there is no way out of these two. But there is a consideration: because they made similarity and difference either an existent attribute or a state, and in both cases it subsists only in the existent. As for the first, because the existence of an attribute is a branch of the existence of the described; and as for the second, because the state subsists only in the existent. So their application [of similarity and difference] to states is in another sense; so the judgment that states are not described by them in the first sense is not ignorance. Then Imam al-Razi, after refuting the two mentioned aspects in the answer, answered the words of the negators: that the state—i.e., its concept—is not a state, but rather a negation (salb), because its meaning is 'being neither existent nor non-existent,' and every concept in which negation is considered is non-existent, not a state. This answer only works if it is claimed that the concept of a state is a state; then it is answered with another answer: that the concept of a state is common between itself and the particular states, so the concept of a state does not have a state additional to itself such that an infinite regress occurs. And if it is claimed that the distinguishing particularities of some states from others are also states, then that answer does not work unless it is said that the particularities are also negations. Know that the investigations related to the existence of the non-existent and the state are corrupt rulings built on false principles, so we have refrained from elaborating on them and wasting time on their justifications.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a complex dialectical argument concerning the nature of 'states' (ahwal) and the possibility of infinite regress. It begins with the assertion that infinite regress is impossible in existing things, but the proponents of states (the Ash'aris) are committed to infinite regress in states, which they claim does not contradict the impossibility in existents, because states are not existent. Al-Jurjani then presents a second argument: states cannot be described as similar or different; thus one cannot say they share in being states (haliyya) because that would imply similarity, nor that they are distinguished by particularities because that would imply difference. Imam al-Razi responds that this is ignorance, since any two mental conceptions are either identical (similarity) or not (difference), so there is no escape. The commentator rebuts by noting that similarity and difference are either attributes or states, and both subsist only in existents, so applying them to states is in a different sense; the original objection is not valid. The passage then moves to al-Razi's second answer: the concept of a 'state' is itself not a state but a negation ('neither existent nor non-existent'), and any concept involving negation is non-existent. The commentator examines this: if the concept of state is itself a state, then it would require another state and lead to infinite regress, but al-Razi avoids that by saying the concept is common between itself and particular states, so no additional state is needed. If particularities are claimed to be states, then they must also be negations. The passage concludes by dismissing the whole inquiry as corrupt and built on false principles, advising against elaboration.

Key Terms
أحوال
سلب
معدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: States are existent entities like attributes.
Correction: The passage clarifies that states are not existent; they are negations.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what is the meaning of the concept of a state?
A) An existent attribute.
B) Being neither existent nor non-existent.
C) A property of the soul.
D) A kind of relation.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari 'state' is not an existent entity but a mental negation, defined as neither existent nor non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes, do you ever unconsciously treat the conceptual distinctions in your mind as if they represent separate realities in God? How does learning that 'states' are mere mental negations challenge you to unify your understanding of God's essence and attributes?
?
This lesson forces you to examine the limits of human language and concepts. Is there an attribute of God that you are tempted to describe as 'something' rather than accept it as a negation? Are you willing to let go of that mental picture and embrace a purely negative definition to avoid misrepresenting God?

Infinite Regress in States vs. Existents
Al-Jurjani presents the argument for the permissibility of infinite regress in states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why infinite regress is considered impossible in existing things but permissible in states, according to the Ash'ari position.

Core Idea
Infinite regress in states does not contradict its impossibility in existents.

A key objection to the Ash'ari theory of states is that it leads to infinite regress. This passage argues that infinite regress is only impossible in existent entities, not in non-existent states, just as it is allowed in relations and privations.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage that states the impossibility of infinite regress in existents but not in states.
Notice the comparison with relations and privations.
2
Identify the key distinction
Understand that the impossibility applies only to existent things, not to non-existent mental categories.
States are not existent.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this defends the Ash'ari position against the charge of inconsistency.
The objection is neutralized by the ontological difference.

First Observatory - Seventh Purpose: Conclusion. On the impossibility of infinite regress in existing things, and their commitment [to it] does not contradict this impossibility; because it is possible that infinite regress is impossible in existents but not impossible in states (ahwal) which are not existent, just as it is not impossible in relations (idafat) and privations (sulub) by agreement. And the second: that states are not described by similarity and difference, so it is not correct to say that they share in being states (haliyya), because that is describing them with similarity, nor that they are distinguished by their particularities, because that is describing them with difference. And Imam al-Razi answered him also by saying that this is ignorance; because any two things that the mind points to in some respect: either the conception of one is the conception of the other or not. On the first, there is similarity between them; on the second, there is difference; there is no way out of these two. But there is a consideration: because they made similarity and difference either an existent attribute or a state, and in both cases it subsists only in the existent. As for the first, because the existence of an attribute is a branch of the existence of the described; and as for the second, because the state subsists only in the existent. So their application [of similarity and difference] to states is in another sense; so the judgment that states are not described by them in the first sense is not ignorance. Then Imam al-Razi, after refuting the two mentioned aspects in the answer, answered the words of the negators: that the state—i.e., its concept—is not a state, but rather a negation (salb), because its meaning is 'being neither existent nor non-existent,' and every concept in which negation is considered is non-existent, not a state. Commentary
This passage is a complex dialectical argument concerning the nature of 'states' (ahwal) and the possibility of infinite regress. It begins with the assertion that infinite regress is impossible in existing things, but the proponents of states (the Ash'aris) are committed to infinite regress in states, which they claim does not contradict the impossibility in existents, because states are not existent. Al-Jurjani then presents a second argument: states cannot be described as similar or different; thus one cannot say they share in being states (haliyya) because that would imply similarity, nor that they are distinguished by particularities because that would imply difference. Imam al-Razi responds that this is ignorance, since any two mental conceptions are either identical (similarity) or not (difference), so there is no escape. The commentator rebuts by noting that similarity and difference are either attributes or states, and both subsist only in existents, so applying them to states is in a different sense; the original objection is not valid. The passage then moves to al-Razi's second answer: the concept of a 'state' is itself not a state but a negation ('neither existent nor non-existent'), and any concept involving negation is non-existent. The commentator examines this: if the concept of state is itself a state, then it would require another state and lead to infinite regress, but al-Razi avoids that by saying the concept is common between itself and particular states, so no additional state is needed. If particularities are claimed to be states, then they must also be negations. The passage concludes by dismissing the whole inquiry as corrupt and built on false principles, advising against elaboration.

The Similarity and Difference of States
Al-Jurjani presents the objection and al-Razi's first response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that states cannot be described as similar or different, and Imam al-Razi's counterargument based on mental conception.

Core Idea
Every two mental concepts are either identical or different, so states must be similar or different.

A second objection to the theory of states is that they cannot be compared in terms of similarity or difference. This lesson presents that objection and al-Razi's response: any two things considered by the mind are either the same or different, so the objection is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the part where it says states cannot be described as similar or different.
Note the two specific claims: sharing haliyya implies similarity, and distinguishing by particularities implies difference.
2
Read al-Razi's response
Read al-Razi's characterization of this objection as ignorance.
He uses the principle of mental conception.
3
Analyze the logic
Understand why al-Razi says there is no escape: every two things are either identical or different.
This is a logical dichotomy.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this response defends the applicability of similarity/difference to states.
States are not exempt from basic logic.

المرصد الأول - المقصد السابع : خاتمة على امتناع التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة، والتزامهم لا ينافي هذا الامتناع ؛ ( لجواز أن يمتنع التسلسل في الموجودات، ولا يمتنع في الأحوال التي ليست بموجودة، (كما لا يمتنع في الإضافات والسلوب) اتفاقا. (والثاني: أن الأحوال لا توصف بالتماثل والاختلاف)، فلا يصح أن يقال : إنها مشتركة في الحالية؛ لأنه وصف لها بالتماثل ولا أنها متمايزة بخصوصياتها؛ لأنه وصف لها بالاختلاف . ( وأجاب ) الإمام الرازي ( عنه ( أيضا بأن ذلك جهالة ؛ لأن كل أمرين يشير إليهما العقل بوجه من الوجوه إما أن يكون المتصور من أحدهما هو المتصور من الآخر أولا، فعلى الأول بينهما تماثل، وعلى الثاني اختلاف فلا مخرج عنهما . ( وفيه نظر لأنهم جعلوا التماثل والاختلاف : إما صفة موجودة ( أو حالاً وعلى كلا التقديرين لا يقوم إلا بالموجود، أما على الأول فلان وجود الصفة فرع وجود الموصوف، وأما على المعتمد قوله : ( والتزامهم إلخ) يعني التزامهم التسلسل في الأحوال لا ينافي امتناعه في الأمور الموجودة، وما قاله الشارح في حواشي شرح التجريد من أن برهان التطبيق يدل على امتناع ترتب أمور غير متناهية مجتمعة في الثبوت، سواء كانت موجودات أو أحوالاً، وهذا البرهان هو في إبطال حوادث لا أول لها، وإثبات الصانع فمراد الإمام أن تجويز التسلسل في الأحوال يسد باب إثبات الصانع بالطريق الذي اعتمدوا عليه فمدفوع بأن قولهم بالمعدومات الثابتة الغير المتناهية مع جريان التطبيق فيها، إذ الترتب ليس بشرط فيه عندهم يوجب سد باب إثبات الصانع، بناء على اشتراط الوجود في جريانه، فكيف التزام التسلسل في الأحوال يوجب ذلك . قوله : (كما لا يمتنع إلخ الأولى تركه ؛ إذ الإضافات والسلوب وجودها بحسب اعتبار العقل، فإذا اعتبرها تسلسلت، وإذا لم يعتبرها انقطعت، بخلاف الأحوال لأنها ثابتة في أنفسها، وليس ثبوتها باعتبار العقل . قوله : ( بينهما تماثل أي في ذلك المتصور . قوله فلا مخرج عنهما إذ لا واسطة بين النقيضين. قوله : ( لأنهم جعلوا إلخ) منع صاحب المقاصد هذا الجعل فلا بد له من شاهد من قوله : (موجودة) قيد بذلك لأن الصفة المعدومة تقوم بالمعدوم . كلامهم . على امتناع ترتيب أمور غير متناهية مجتمعة في الثبوت سواء كانت موجودات أو أحوالاً. وهذا البرهان هو المعتمد عندهم في إبطال حوادث لا أول لها وإثبات الصانع، فمراد الإمام أن تجويز التسلسل في الأحوال يسد باب إثبات الصانع بالطريق الذي سلكوه واعتمدوا عليه، وهذا القدر يكفي إلزاما لهم . قوله : ( لأنه وصف لها بالتماثل حمل التماثل على معناه الاصطلاحي حتى يتوقف على كون الحالية من أخص الصفات النفسية وهو محل بحث وحمله على معناه اللغوي لا يتوقف عليه، لكن في كونه من الأحوال تردد وبالجملة مراد الناقض بالاشتراك والاختلاف معناهما اللغويان، والأحوال بل المعدومات أيضاً توصف بهما، فجواب الإمام حق ولا يرد نظر المصنف . الثاني فلأن الحال لا يقوم إلا بالموجود ) فإطلاقهما ) أي إطلاق التماثل والاختلاف على الأحوال يكون بمعنى آخر؛ فلا يكون الحكم بأن الأحوال لا توصف بهما بالمعنى الأول جهالة . ثم إن الإمام الرازي بعد ما زيف الوجهين المذكورين في الجواب (أجاب) عن كلام النافين ( بأن الحال أي مفهومه (ليس حالاً، بل هو سلب إذ معناه كونه ليس موجوداً ولا معدوماً وكل مفهوم اعتبر فيه سلب كان معدوماً لا حالا . وهذا الجواب إنما يتمشى إذا ادعي أن مفهوم الحال حال، وحينئذ يجاب بجواب آخر أيضاً ، وهو أن مفهوم الحال مشترك بين نفسه والأحوال الخاصة، فلا يكون لمفهوم الحال حال زائد على نفسه حتى يتسلسل وأما إذا ادعي أن الخصوصيات المميزة لبعض الأحوال عن بعض أحوال أيضاً، فلا يتم ذلك الجواب إلا إذا قيل : إن الخصوصيات أيضا سلوب. واعلم أن المباحث المتعلقة بثبوت المعدوم والحال أحكام فاسدة مبنية على أصول باطلة فلذلك أعرضنا عن الإطناب فيها وتضييع الأوقات في توجيهاتها .
First Observatory - Seventh Purpose: Conclusion. On the impossibility of infinite regress in existing things, and their commitment [to it] does not contradict this impossibility; because it is possible that infinite regress is impossible in existents but not impossible in states (ahwal) which are not existent, just as it is not impossible in relations (idafat) and privations (sulub) by agreement. And the second: that states are not described by similarity and difference, so it is not correct to say that they share in being states (haliyya), because that is describing them with similarity, nor that they are distinguished by their particularities, because that is describing them with difference. And Imam al-Razi answered him also by saying that this is ignorance; because any two things that the mind points to in some respect: either the conception of one is the conception of the other or not. On the first, there is similarity between them; on the second, there is difference; there is no way out of these two. But there is a consideration: because they made similarity and difference either an existent attribute or a state, and in both cases it subsists only in the existent. As for the first, because the existence of an attribute is a branch of the existence of the described; and as for the second, because the state subsists only in the existent. So their application [of similarity and difference] to states is in another sense; so the judgment that states are not described by them in the first sense is not ignorance. Then Imam al-Razi, after refuting the two mentioned aspects in the answer, answered the words of the negators: that the state—i.e., its concept—is not a state, but rather a negation (salb), because its meaning is 'being neither existent nor non-existent,' and every concept in which negation is considered is non-existent, not a state. This answer only works if it is claimed that the concept of a state is a state; then it is answered with another answer: that the concept of a state is common between itself and the particular states, so the concept of a state does not have a state additional to itself such that an infinite regress occurs. And if it is claimed that the distinguishing particularities of some states from others are also states, then that answer does not work unless it is said that the particularities are also negations. Know that the investigations related to the existence of the non-existent and the state are corrupt rulings built on false principles, so we have refrained from elaborating on them and wasting time on their justifications.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a complex dialectical argument concerning the nature of 'states' (ahwal) and the possibility of infinite regress. It begins with the assertion that infinite regress is impossible in existing things, but the proponents of states (the Ash'aris) are committed to infinite regress in states, which they claim does not contradict the impossibility in existents, because states are not existent. Al-Jurjani then presents a second argument: states cannot be described as similar or different; thus one cannot say they share in being states (haliyya) because that would imply similarity, nor that they are distinguished by particularities because that would imply difference. Imam al-Razi responds that this is ignorance, since any two mental conceptions are either identical (similarity) or not (difference), so there is no escape. The commentator rebuts by noting that similarity and difference are either attributes or states, and both subsist only in existents, so applying them to states is in a different sense; the original objection is not valid. The passage then moves to al-Razi's second answer: the concept of a 'state' is itself not a state but a negation ('neither existent nor non-existent'), and any concept involving negation is non-existent. The commentator examines this: if the concept of state is itself a state, then it would require another state and lead to infinite regress, but al-Razi avoids that by saying the concept is common between itself and particular states, so no additional state is needed. If particularities are claimed to be states, then they must also be negations. The passage concludes by dismissing the whole inquiry as corrupt and built on false principles, advising against elaboration.

Key Terms
تماثل
اختلاف
حالية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi say about any two things the mind points to?
A) They are always different.
B) They are always similar.
C) They are either identical or different.
D) They are neither similar nor different.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi argues that similarity and difference are exhaustive categories for mental conceptions, so states must be comparable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been in a debate where you assumed two things were neither the same nor different? How does this lesson expose the flaw in that reasoning, and how can you apply it in your own thinking about Allah's attributes?
?
When you consider your own understanding of tawhid, do you fall into the trap of thinking that some aspects are beyond logical scrutiny? What would al-Razi say to you, and how will you correct yourself?
?
This lesson shows that identity and difference are exhaustive for mental concepts. Is there a belief you hold that you've never subjected to this logical test? Will you now examine it, and what might you find?

The Rebuttal and the Second Answer
The commentator rebuts al-Razi's first response and presents al-Razi's second answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rebuttal to al-Razi's first response and his alternative solution that the concept of a state is a negation.

Core Idea
Similarity and difference subsist only in existents; al-Razi's second answer defines the state concept as a negation.

After presenting his first response, the commentator offers a rebuttal: similarity and difference, whether as attributes or states, require an existent subject. Al-Razi then provides a second, more refined answer: the very concept of a state is a negation, thus not subject to the same logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the rebuttal
Read the part that says similarity and difference subsist only in existents.
This challenges al-Razi's first response.
2
Understand the implication
Because states are not existent, similarity/difference apply in a different sense, so the original objection is not refuted.
3
Read al-Razi's second answer
Read where al-Razi says the concept of a state is a negation.
This is his definitive answer.
4
Reflect on the solution
Consider how defining the concept of state as a negation avoids the problem of infinite regress and similarity/difference.
The concept itself is not a state.

First Observatory - Seventh Purpose: Conclusion. On the impossibility of infinite regress in existing things, and their commitment [to it] does not contradict this impossibility; because it is possible that infinite regress is impossible in existents but not impossible in states (ahwal) which are not existent, just as it is not impossible in relations (idafat) and privations (sulub) by agreement. And the second: that states are not described by similarity and difference, so it is not correct to say that they share in being states (haliyya), because that is describing them with similarity, nor that they are distinguished by their particularities, because that is describing them with difference. And Imam al-Razi answered him also by saying that this is ignorance; because any two things that the mind points to in some respect: either the conception of one is the conception of the other or not. On the first, there is similarity between them; on the second, there is difference; there is no way out of these two. But there is a consideration: because they made similarity and difference either an existent attribute or a state, and in both cases it subsists only in the existent. As for the first, because the existence of an attribute is a branch of the existence of the described; and as for the second, because the state subsists only in the existent. So their application [of similarity and difference] to states is in another sense; so the judgment that states are not described by them in the first sense is not ignorance. Then Imam al-Razi, after refuting the two mentioned aspects in the answer, answered the words of the negators: that the state—i.e., its concept—is not a state, but rather a negation (salb), because its meaning is 'being neither existent nor non-existent,' and every concept in which negation is considered is non-existent, not a state. This answer only works if it is claimed that the concept of a state is a state; then it is answered with another answer: that the concept of a state is common between itself and the particular states, so the concept of a state does not have a state additional to itself such that an infinite regress occurs. And if it is claimed that the distinguishing particularities of some states from others are also states, then that answer does not work unless it is said that the particularities are also negations. Know that the investigations related to the existence of the non-existent and the state are corrupt rulings built on false principles, so we have refrained from elaborating on them and wasting time on their justifications.

Commentary
This passage is a complex dialectical argument concerning the nature of 'states' (ahwal) and the possibility of infinite regress. It begins with the assertion that infinite regress is impossible in existing things, but the proponents of states (the Ash'aris) are committed to infinite regress in states, which they claim does not contradict the impossibility in existents, because states are not existent. Al-Jurjani then presents a second argument: states cannot be described as similar or different; thus one cannot say they share in being states (haliyya) because that would imply similarity, nor that they are distinguished by particularities because that would imply difference. Imam al-Razi responds that this is ignorance, since any two mental conceptions are either identical (similarity) or not (difference), so there is no escape. The commentator rebuts by noting that similarity and difference are either attributes or states, and both subsist only in existents, so applying them to states is in a different sense; the original objection is not valid. The passage then moves to al-Razi's second answer: the concept of a 'state' is itself not a state but a negation ('neither existent nor non-existent'), and any concept involving negation is non-existent. The commentator examines this: if the concept of state is itself a state, then it would require another state and lead to infinite regress, but al-Razi avoids that by saying the concept is common between itself and particular states, so no additional state is needed. If particularities are claimed to be states, then they must also be negations. The passage concludes by dismissing the whole inquiry as corrupt and built on false principles, advising against elaboration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The concept of a state must be a state itself.
Correction: Al-Razi argues the concept is a negation, not a state.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's second answer, what is the concept of a 'state'?
A) An existent attribute.
B) A state that subsists in the mind.
C) A negation (salb).
D) A relation (idafa).

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's definitive answer is that the concept of a state is a negation, thereby avoiding the problems of infinite regress and similarity/difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you treat your own mental concepts—like 'state' or 'attribute'—as if they are real things, causing unnecessary confusion in your understanding of God? Will you now commit to examining each concept to see if it is a real existent or merely a negation?
?
Do you tend to settle for defensive answers (like al-Razi's first response) when challenged, or do you push yourself to find the deeper clarification (like the second answer)? How will you cultivate the habit of seeking the root of an objection rather than just deflecting it?
?
What 'states' in your own theology have you mistakenly considered real entities? How will you re-evaluate them as simple negations to avoid logical pitfalls in your faith?

Al-Rāzī's Answer: The Concept of State as Negation
Al-Rāzī presents his answer to the negators' objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Rāzī's argument that the concept of a state is a negation, not a state, and how this addresses the objection about similarity and difference.

Core Idea
The concept of a state is a negation, so it cannot be described as similar or different.

Al-Rāzī responds to the objection that states cannot be described as similar or different by redefining the concept of a state itself. He argues that the state (al-ḥāl) is not a real entity but a negation (salb) — neither existent nor non-existent. This lesson unpacks his answer and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from al-Rāzī
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how al-Rāzī structures his response: first the second point, then his main answer, then an alternative.
2
Identify the core claim
Identify the statement 'the state is not a state, but a negation.' Understand what negation means here.
Negation (salb) is a key term.
3
Understand the two scenarios
Al-Rāzī considers two possibilities: if the concept of state is claimed to be a state, and if it is not. See the different answers.
4
Reflect on the implications
How does this answer protect the doctrine of states from the objection? Think about why denying similarity/difference is not ignorance under this view.
Also note the remark about corrupt investigations.

الثاني فلأن الحال لا يقوم إلا بالموجود ) فإطلاقهما ) أي إطلاق التماثل والاختلاف على الأحوال يكون بمعنى آخر؛ فلا يكون الحكم بأن الأحوال لا توصف بهما بالمعنى الأول جهالة . ثم إن الإمام الرازي بعد ما زيف الوجهين المذكورين في الجواب (أجاب) عن كلام النافين ( بأن الحال أي مفهومه (ليس حالاً، بل هو سلب إذ معناه كونه ليس موجوداً ولا معدوماً وكل مفهوم اعتبر فيه سلب كان معدوماً لا حالا . وهذا الجواب إنما يتمشى إذا ادعي أن مفهوم الحال حال، وحينئذ يجاب بجواب آخر أيضاً ، وهو أن مفهوم الحال مشترك بين نفسه والأحوال الخاصة، فلا يكون لمفهوم الحال حال زائد على نفسه حتى يتسلسل وأما إذا ادعي أن الخصوصيات المميزة لبعض الأحوال عن بعض أحوال أيضاً، فلا يتم ذلك الجواب إلا إذا قيل : إن الخصوصيات أيضا سلوب. واعلم أن المباحث المتعلقة بثبوت المعدوم والحال أحكام فاسدة مبنية على أصول باطلة فلذلك أعرضنا عن الإطناب فيها وتضييع الأوقات في توجيهاتها .
أن
قوله : فلا يكون الحكم إلخ هذه الجهالة وإن اندفعت لكن بقي جهالة أخرى، وهي المعلل أثبت زيادة الحالية باشتراك الأحوال فيها وامتيازها بالخصوصيات لا بالتماثل والاختلاف بالمعنى المذكور، فالجواب بأنها لا توصف بالتماثل والاختلاف جهالة بينة. فالحاصل : أنهم إن أرادوا بالتماثل والاختلاف مجرد الاشتراك والتباين فنفيهما عن الأحوال جهالة، وإن أرادوا معنى أخص منهما، فالجواب بعدم اتصافها بهما جهالة .
=
قوله: (أجاب إلخ) هذا الجواب مندفع بما حررنا لك، إذ اختصاص الاتصاف به حال الحالية ينافي كونه معدوماً، فعلم أن السلب ليس داخلاً في مفهومه، بل خارج عنه لازم له، وحقيقته المفهوم المتحقق تبعا . قوله : ( كان معدوماً) بناء على أن عدم الجزء يستلزم عدم الكل، بل عينه، وبهذا ظهر فساد تجويز شارح التجريد تقوم الحال بالمعدوم بناء على أنه لم يبلغ حد الوجود، كما جوزوا تقوم الموجود بالحال بناء على أنه خرج من حد العدم. قوله: (مشترك بين نفسه والأحوال ) وامتيازها عنه بقيد سلبي، وهو أن حاليته ليست زائدة
على نفسه .
قوله : ( فلأن الحال لا يقوم إلا بالموجود فيه بحث : لأن القيام في الجملة كاف كما مر في الجوهرية. وتماثل الموجودين واختلافهما قائمان به فلا يقدح في كون التماثل والاختلاف من الأحوال قيامها بالأحوال في الجملة.
قوله : ( وكل مفهوم اعتبر فيه سلب إلخ ) فيه دفع لرد الفاضل الطوسي على جواب الإمام بأن الحال وصف ليس بموجود ولا معدوم، فلا يكون سلباً محضاً . وحاصل الدفع : أن اعتبار السلب في مفهوم الحال ولو بالجزئية يستلزم عدميته ولاحاجة بنا إلى ادعاء أن هذا السلب عين مفهوم الحال .
The second: because the state (al-ḥāl) does not subsist except in an existent. So their application—i.e., the application of similarity (al-tamāthul) and difference (al-ikhtilāf) to states—has another meaning; thus the judgment that states are not described by them in the first meaning is not ignorance. Then Imam al-Rāzī, after refuting the two mentioned aspects in the reply, answered the statement of the negators by saying: the state (al-ḥāl)—i.e., its concept—is not a state; rather it is a negation (salb), for its meaning is that it is neither existent nor non-existent, and every concept in which a negation is considered is non-existent, not a state. This reply proceeds only if it is claimed that the concept of state is a state; then another reply is also given: that the concept of state is common between itself and the particular states, so the concept of state does not have a state additional to itself such that a regress would occur. But if it is claimed that the distinguishing particularities of some states from others are also states, then that reply does not work unless it is said that the particularities are also negations. And know that the investigations concerning the subsistence of the non-existent and the state are corrupt judgments built on false principles, so we have refrained from elaborating on them and wasting time on their justifications.
That his statement: 'So the judgment, etc.' This ignorance, even if it is repelled, still another ignorance remains: that the reasoner has established the additional statehood (al-ḥāliyya) by the common participation of states in it and their distinction by particularities, not by similarity and difference in the mentioned meaning. So the reply that they are not described by similarity and difference is clear ignorance. The conclusion: if by similarity and difference they mean mere commonality and distinctness, then denying them of states is ignorance; if they mean a more specific meaning, then the reply that they are not qualified by them is ignorance.
=
His statement: '(He answered, etc.)' This reply is refuted by what we have established for you, for the specific quality of being qualified by it being a state of statehood (ḥāl al-ḥāliyya) contradicts its being non-existent; thus it is known that negation is not part of its concept, but rather external to it and necessary for it, and its reality is the concept realized dependently. His statement: '(He was non-existent)' based on the view that the non-existence of a part entails the non-existence of the whole, indeed it is identical to it. By this the corruption of the commentator on al-Tajrīd allowing the state to subsist in the non-existent appears—based on the view that it has not reached the level of existence—just as they allowed the existent to subsist in the state based on the view that it has left the level of non-existence. His statement: '(Common between itself and the states)' and its distinction from them by a negative qualification, that its statehood is not additional to itself.
His statement: '(Because the state does not subsist except in an existent, there is discussion: because subsistence in general is sufficient, as has passed in the discussion of substance. And the similarity of two existents and their difference subsist in it, so it does not invalidate that similarity and difference are among the states that they subsist in states in general.)'
His statement: '(And every concept in which a negation is considered, etc.)' contains a repulsion of the reply of al-Fāḍil al-Ṭūsī to the Imam's answer that the state is a description not existent nor non-existent, so it is not pure negation. The gist of the repulsion is that the consideration of negation in the concept of the state, even partially, entails its non-existentiality; and we have no need to claim that this negation is identical to the concept of the state.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a discussion and resolution of an objection regarding the description of states (aḥwāl) with similarity (tamāthul) and difference (ikhtilāf). It begins by presenting a second point: the state only subsists in an existent, so applying similarity and difference to states has a different meaning; thus denying that states are described by these terms in the first sense does not constitute ignorance. Then it reports al-Rāzī's answer to the negators: the concept of a state is not a state but a negation (salb), meaning it is neither existent nor non-existent; any concept involving negation is non-existent, not a state. This answer works if it is assumed that the concept of state is itself a state; otherwise, another answer is given: the concept is common between itself and particular states, so no regress occurs. If the distinguishing particularities are also claimed to be states, then the particularities must be negations. The author then remarks that investigations into the subsistence of the non-existent and the state are corrupt, built on false principles. Next, the commentary (by al-Ṭaftāzānī) addresses a remaining ignorance: the reasoner established additional statehood by common participation and distinction by particularities, not by similarity/difference; so the reply that states are not described by similarity/difference is still ignorance. The conclusion distinguishes two meanings: if similarity/difference mean mere commonality/distinctness, denying them is ignorant; if a more specific meaning is intended, then denying qualification is also ignorant. Then a refutation of al-Rāzī's answer is given: the specific quality of being a state of statehood (ḥāl al-ḥāliyya) contradicts non-existence, so negation is external to the concept, not part of it. The commentary also discusses a point about the state not subsisting except in an existent, arguing that subsistence in general suffices, and similarity/difference of two existents subsist in them, so they can be states. Finally, it repulses al-Ṭūsī's reply to al-Rāzī: considering negation even partially entails non-existentiality, and there is no need to claim the negation is identical to the concept.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that denying similarity and difference to states is always ignorant.
Correction: Al-Rāzī shows that if we understand similarity and difference in their primary sense (applying to existents), then denying them of states is not ignorance because the concept of state is a negation, not an existent.

Checkpoint
According to al-Rāzī, what is the concept of a state?
A) An existent entity
B) A negation (salb)
C) A particularity
D) A substance

Key Takeaway
Al-Rāzī defends the doctrine of states by arguing that the concept of a state is a negation, not a state, thus avoiding the objection about similarity and difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that everything must be either real or unreal, without considering a middle ground? How does al-Rāzī's concept of state as a negation challenge that binary thinking? What in your life might be similar—something that is neither simply true nor false but a conceptual tool? Commit to examining one such assumption today.
?
Al-Rāzī warns against wasting time on corrupt investigations built on false principles. Are there debates or intellectual pursuits in your life that are based on flawed foundations? How can you identify and abandon them, redirecting your energy to beneficial knowledge?
?
The concept of state is defined by what it is not. Is there an area of your faith or understanding that you define more by negation than by positive affirmation? How might that be limiting your spiritual growth? Resolve to seek positive knowledge that builds certainty.

Refining the Response: Similarity, Difference, and the Reality of States
The commentary refines al-Rāzī's answer and critiques it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentary's critique of al-Rāzī's answer and the refined position on whether states can be described as similar or different.

Core Idea
Whether states are described as similar or different depends on the meaning of those terms.

The commentary (Shaykh al-Ṭaftāzānī) examines al-Rāzī's answer more closely. It identifies a remaining ignorance: the reasoner established statehood through common participation and particularities, not through similarity and difference. The commentary then distinguishes two meanings of similarity/difference and refines the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the commentator's critique and distinction.
2
Identify the 'remaining ignorance'
Find where the commentator points out that even if al-Rāzī's answer resolves one issue, another ignorance remains.
3
Understand the two meanings
The commentator distinguishes between similarity/difference as mere commonality/distinctness and a more specific meaning.
4
Analyze the refutation of al-Rāzī
The commentator refutes al-Rāzī's claim that negation is part of the concept. Understand the argument that negation is external.
Also note the discussion on subsistence.

Commentary
This passage is a discussion and resolution of an objection regarding the description of states (aḥwāl) with similarity (tamāthul) and difference (ikhtilāf). It begins by presenting a second point: the state only subsists in an existent, so applying similarity and difference to states has a different meaning; thus denying that states are described by these terms in the first sense does not constitute ignorance. Then it reports al-Rāzī's answer to the negators: the concept of a state is not a state but a negation (salb), meaning it is neither existent nor non-existent; any concept involving negation is non-existent, not a state. This answer works if it is assumed that the concept of state is itself a state; otherwise, another answer is given: the concept is common between itself and particular states, so no regress occurs. If the distinguishing particularities are also claimed to be states, then the particularities must be negations. The author then remarks that investigations into the subsistence of the non-existent and the state are corrupt, built on false principles. Next, the commentary (by al-Ṭaftāzānī) addresses a remaining ignorance: the reasoner established additional statehood by common participation and distinction by particularities, not by similarity/difference; so the reply that states are not described by similarity/difference is still ignorance. The conclusion distinguishes two meanings: if similarity/difference mean mere commonality/distinctness, denying them is ignorant; if a more specific meaning is intended, then denying qualification is also ignorant. Then a refutation of al-Rāzī's answer is given: the specific quality of being a state of statehood (ḥāl al-ḥāliyya) contradicts non-existence, so negation is external to the concept, not part of it. The commentary also discusses a point about the state not subsisting except in an existent, arguing that subsistence in general suffices, and similarity/difference of two existents subsist in them, so they can be states. Finally, it repulses al-Ṭūsī's reply to al-Rāzī: considering negation even partially entails non-existentiality, and there is no need to claim the negation is identical to the concept.

According to the commentary, what is the 'remaining ignorance' in al-Rāzī's answer?
A) The state is not a negation

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological answer that seems to resolve a difficulty, do you ever pause to ask 'What do they really mean by these terms?' — as the commentator did with 'similarity and difference'? What prevents you from doing so? Will you commit to defining key terms before accepting an argument?
?
The commentator points out a 'remaining ignorance' even after al-Rāzī's answer. What is the 'remaining ignorance' in your own understanding of Allah's attributes that you have been content with? Will you now actively seek to uncover it, or will you remain satisfied with surface-level answers?
?
Consider the claim that negation is external to the concept of state. This lesson shows that even a partial negation affects the nature of a concept. Is there an area of your creed where you have allowed a 'partial negation' (like denying an attribute) without realizing its implications? Are you ready to investigate that now?
